MILLER DRAMATICALLY ACCUSED OF MURDER BEFORE A JUSTICE.
Incidents followed closely upon each other In exciting succession yesterday at Sulsun and Fairfield. The exclusive publication In The Call of the sensational arrest of W. J. Miller for the murder of
Daniel H. Wilson created a profound sensation. The excitement was Increased to fever heat when the Sheriff of Solano and his deputies placed Mrs. E. G. Dickinson, the mother of Miller,
and Mrs. Addle Wilson, the sister of the accused man, under arrest upon charges of murder. The women were taken from the McEwen ranch and in a pelting rain were driven down the
SulÃun road to the County Jail at Fairfleld. In the afternoon the three accused persons were formally charged before a Justice of the Peace.

crowd around them was suspiciously

Finally the Sheriff entered and quietly

struck. Her lips twitched and her eyes

agony of the animal. Finally the
woman controlled herself. "Well," she
said, tremblingly, "you have me now."
Mm. Dickinson is an aged woman, a
great-grandmother, as she now faces

When Mrs. Wilson was formally ac

Her lips closed in silent defiance, and

The women were told that they could

After the women had been formally
accused and arrested, the same process
was submitted to Miller, who again
proclaimed his Innocence and his ability

Miller was then taken back to Jail
and the exciting incidents of the day
were over. The accused mother and
her children will stake their hope of life
and liberty on the ability of the son to
establish an alibi. Miller claims that
he can satisfactorily explain all of his
movements on the day and night of the
tragedy. He Insists that he hired a
buggy at Vallejo two days after the
murder and at no other time. The en
tire case will hinge upon, this incident
and upon it will depend the life of the
accused man and perhaps the lives of
his mother and sister.

nobody in this world can say that I

"When this case first came up my

Did old man Wilson have so much

No, sir. I would not stain my hands

And neither would any of my family.

He probably deserved his fate. No

"The officers must think they have

Well, I don't care, I didn't do the job,

"My wife went cut the night of the

9 o'clock. I was in the house with the

As Jailer Tom Linihan ushered The

Mrs. Dickinson made a supreme effort

"I have very little to say about this

officers are on the wrong track to dis
over the murderer of Dan Wilson. My
son had no hand in the murder. Neither
did I or my daughter or Mr. McEwen.
The night of the murder none of us
left the house. About 8 o'clock, the
time the murder is supposed to have
been committed, I was undressed and
in bed. Mr. McEwen was still down
stairs. If I remember correctly he was
winding the clock. Mrs. Wilson was
sleeping with her daughter in the
cabin. The first we heard of the mur
der was when two men came to the
house and awakened us out of a sound
sleep. We heard the dogs barking
furiously and when the men arrived at
the house we got up. They told us
that Dan "Wilson had been murdered,
and we at once suspected one of the
women with whom he lived. That's
all I know about the case, and that's
all I will know if they keep me in this
dirty place the rest of my life. That is
the gospel truth, and I couldn't tell
any more if I wished. They will have
to look outside of my family for the
murderer of Dan Wilson."
Miller's Movements Are
Now Traced in Vallejo
VALLEJO, Jan. 11.â The arrest of W.
J. Miller at Port Costa for the brutal
murder of old Dan Wilson two years
ago, the exclusive account of which
was published in The Call this morn
ing, created a sensation in this city,
partly because Miller was a resident of
Vallejo at the time the crime was com
mitted and partly because a number
of the principal witnesses in the case
against him still live here. The story
of the murder was almost the Bole topic
of discussion on the street? all day, and
various opinions were advanced as to
the probable guilt of the accused.
The consensus of opinion seems to- be
that the evidence againat Miller Is so
PRICE FIVE CENTS.
strong that a solution of the mysterious
tragedy that has balked the best efforts
of the authorities for the last two years
to unravel is at last in a fair way of
being realized. There is at least one
family in Vallejo, however, to whom
the news of Miller's arrest came as an
unpleasant surprise.
The Sheehans, on whom Miller de
pends to prove that he was at home on
the night and at the hour when the as
sassin's bullet ended the existence of
the aged rancher, were greatly wrought
up at the possibility of being called up
on to go on the witness stand. Their
trepidation could hardly have been
greater if they had themselves been
charged with the crime. Every caller
at the house was closely scrutinized
through closed blinds or a crack in the
door. A reporter for The Call who vis
ited the Sheehan residence to-night was
challenged before he could open the
gate.
The entire family was gathered in the
front hall peering through the door,
â which was opened about three inches.
"What do you want? We have nothing
to say," was the greeting in excited
tones, and slain went the door before
the reporter could state his mission.
The evident apprehension of the Shee
! hans would seem to Indicate that they
J are in a quandary and do not know
which way to turn to get out of a tight
place. Whether they will stick to their
; original story when put to the test and
thereby help to strengthen the alibi
which Miller claims he will prove re
i mains to be seen.

distinctly the circumstance, but as he
was not acquainted with Miller, and as
the man who hired the rig was muffled
in a heavy ulster, he could not identify
him.

FINAL AGREEMENT ON
THE HAWAIIAN BILL
But Democrats Reserve the Right to
Make a Minority Report Op
posing Several Features.
WASHINGTON. Jan. 11.â The House
Committee on Territories reached a final
agreement this afternoon to make a fa
vorable report on the Hawaiian bill. The
agreement to report was unanimous, al
though the Democrats of the committee
reserved the right to make a minority re
port opposing several features of the bill.
The drafting of the majority report was
left to Chairman Knox, wno will take
several days before presenting the bill
and report.
As agreed to the bill is in all essential
features the bill submitted by the Ha
waiian Commission, although changed to
conform to changes already made by the
Senate committee, and also as a result of
the discussions before the committee, cov
ering more than a month. Most of the
Senate amendments are agreed to, the
purpose being to have the legislation in
both houses conform as far as possible.
In addition to these, section 25 of the
committee bill is struck out. It provided
puniÃhment of persons guilty of disre
spect or offense against the legislative
bodies of Hawaii.
Another change, not made by the Sen
ate, is the striking out of section 75 for
an investigation or the land laws of Ha
waii. The qualifications of electors, Rep
resentatives and Senators, which have
been subject to much discussion, are ar
ranged so that no limitations are placed
on admission to the House, while the
qualifications of Senators and electors of,
Senators are $1000 of property or $600 In
come.
The minority report will be directed
mainly against the principle of a property
qualification and against life tenures for
the judiciary.
ANNUAL MEETING OF THE
AMERICAN SUGAR COMPANY
President Havemeyer Reports Radi
cal Changss Have Been Made, but
Does Not State Their Nature.
NEW YORK, Jan. 11.â The annual meet
in? of the American Sugar Refining Com
pany was held in Jersey City at noon to
ii;iy. President Havemeyer said that it'
had been found necessary to make radi
cal changes in the policy of the compa
ny's business, and that these changes
were made with the unanimous consent
of the full board of directors. No report
was submitted for the year, and no indi-*"
cation of the condition of the business was :
disclosed in the president's remarks. Low
ell M. Palmer was elected a director in
place of John E. Searles, who had de
clined a re-election; John Mayor was
elected to succeed William Dick, resigned,
and John E. Parsons was elected to sue-
ceed himself. Frederick Heicke of Jer
sey City was elected secretary pro tern.
It was resolved that the accumulated
profits, less dividends declared up to Jan
uary 3, be reserved as a working capital.

OWL TRAIN WRECKED
BY A BAND OF SHEEP
Engineer William M. Wood Killed
in a Railway Disaster Near
OAKLAND, Jan. v. â A report was received from In
gomar to-night that the "Owl," as the San Francisco and' Los
Angeles limited is called, had been ditched eight miles this
side of the Los Banos depot, and that Engineer William M.
Wood, aged 35. of West Oakland* was killed. Xews of the
wreck was taken to Los Banos by a messenger, who
had to go through the storm for eight miles to send word to
the mole at West Oakland.
The "Owl" left the pier at 5:24 and was running at the
rate of fifty miles an hour when it struck the curve at Ingo
mar. This was at about 8:30 o'clock to-night. While round
ing this curve the train ran into a band of sheep, and the
locomotive, the smoker and one car were thrown to one side.
There is a sidetrack at this point, and the locomotive tum
bled on its side into a boarding car. The locomotive and
smoker were badly wrecked and the boarding car was
smashed.

terrible catastrophe which occurred at

In the center of lower city (Hang Chow
Is divided into three partsâ the lower, up
per and middle cities) took lire and some
ten minutes after the flames were first
noticed there was an explosion which
could be heard f.>r miles. Frightful loss
of life and property resulted. No less
than 1500 Chinese and two Europeans who
had charge of the factory were instantly
killed ana hundreds were wounded, many
horribly mutilated, and hundreds of
houses were destroyed by the explosion of
hundreds of barrels of newly-made gun
powder.
The whole of the lower city Is in ruins,
ptreet aft. r street being nothing bat a
mass of fallen timbers. The upper and
middle cities were comparatively un
harmed.
The factory is said to have been fired
by the members of a Chinese secret soci
ety, who an- plotting against the govern
ment. A number or arrests are said to
have been made by the Mandarins, and
many executions "will follow.

NAGLE'S SLAYER CAUGHT.
Perpetrator of an Atrocious Crime
Arrested at Fresno.
FRESNO, Jan. 11.â Deputy Sheriff
John White to-day arrested Frank
Brend on a charge of murder, commit
ted at Paulsell Station, in Stanislaus
county. The crime was committed last
Saturday. The man whom Brend is
charged with having killed was Frank
Nagle. They were both section hands
on the Sierra road and had a quarrel
on Saturday morning. After having it
out it was supposed that the whole
thing was over. That night, however,
Brend entered the room where the men
were asleep and fatally shot Nagle in
his couch. He then escaped and the
officers have been looking for him ever
since. When taken into custody he ad
mitted his guilt.

THE SAN FKAKCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, JANUARY 12, 1899.
DREYFUS INQUIRY
ALMOST FINISHED
It Is Said the Court of Cassa=
tion Will Grant Revision
of the Famous Trial.
PARIS, Jan. 11. â M. Quesnay de Beaurepaire, the former presi
dent of the civil section of the Court of Cassation, followÃ up his at
tack on the criminal section of the Court of Cassation to-day in an
article published by the Echo de Paris, in which he insinuates that
Judge Loew, president of the court, went out of his way to appoint
M. Bardo reporter of the Dreyfus affair because he was sure of his
partiality, and that he also appointed "Dreyfusite" magistrates for
other branches of the inquiry. Moreover, AI. Beaurepaire says the
rehabilitation of Colonel Picquart by Judge Loew is tantamount to
an uncalled-for declaration of the innocence of Dreyfus.
Furthermore, M. de Beaurepaire claims that Judge Loew displayed
hostility and aversion to the generals when the latter were testify
ing, and expressed approval of the attacks on the general staff in
the testimony of an ex-officer who was cashiered for intemperance.
The charge is also made that Judge Loew- in examining the chiefs of
the army and the officials of the War Office put the questions with
the object of confusion, and that otherwise he frequently showed
bias.
The Liberte to-day quotes "a personage closely connected with
the Court of Cassations" ns saying that the inquiry into the Drey
fus case is about finished; that a decision may be expected early in
February, and that it will be almost unanimously favorable to a re
vision of the trial.

CONTRACT DECLARED
ILLEGAL AND VOID
Sacramento County Will Not Sue
the State for the Collection
of Back Commissions.
SACRAMENTO, Jan. 11.â The Board of Supervisors this afternoon,
at a special meeting held for the purpose of considering the request Qf
the Chamber of Commerce and others for the rescinding of the contract
with attorneys for the collection of back commissions from the State,
received an opinion from District Attorney Baker holding that the con
tract was illegal. The following resolution introduced by Supervisor
McLaughlin was then adopted:
Whereas, This Board of Supervisors did on the 10th day of September,
1898. pass a resolution employing two firms of attorneys, to wit: Rogers,
Paterson & Slack of San Francisco and Devlin & Devlin of Sacramento City,
to assist the District Attorney of Sacramento County to collect certain com
missions and charges supposed to be owing and unpaid by the State of Cali
fornia to the -county of Sacramento; and,
Whereas, It now appears to us that said resolution so adopted by said
Board as aforesaid, and Bald contract, if any, entered Into by this board with
said firm of attorneys was, and the same is Illegal and void, for the reason
that the public interest of the citizens and taxpayers of Sacramento County
will be prejudiced by permitting said resolution and contract to stand; now,
therefore, be it
Resolved, That .said resolution be and the same is hereby rescinded.
reference to the Nicaraguan canal are
now in such shape that it would be
difficult for a successor to Sir Julian j
to handle them properly. In view of
this fact, Lord Salisbury has directed ]
that he be retained in Washington, and ;
the embassy to-day received official ;
word to this effect.
I learned to-day that Great Britain
does not propose to relinquish without
compensation the rights conferred upon j
her in the proposed canal by the j
Clayton-Bulwer treaty. The conces- '
sions she wants have been outlined to I
Secretary Hay along with her an- I
nouncement of her willingness to com- i
ply with the wishes of this Govern- j
ment for the abrogation of the Clayton-
Bulwer convention. But she desires It
to be understood that the canal must
be neutral in war as well aB in peace,
for she appreciates the importance of
such a waterway in case of hostilities
in the far East.
The administration is quite willing
that the canal shall be strictly neutral
and there will be no difficulty, it 1b ex
pected, in reaching a satisfactory con
clusion on this point. Great Britnln
will probably want concessions relative I
to canal charges and other minor
points, but it is believed that neutrality
of the waterway is with her the most
important question.

EATON THE ANGEL
CITY'S PARKHURST
Los Angeles' Mayor to Begin a
Crusade Against Saloons
That Admit Women.
LOS ANGELES, Jan. 11.â The new Mayor of Los Angeles, Fred
Eaton, has some well denned ideas as to what a saloon should be, and
it is his intention to so use his influence that these places shall be
conducted according to his ideas. He has set hie foot down on the
resorts that cater to the trade of women, and is now preparing for
a crusade.
"I am not opposed to the legitimate Baloon," said the Mayor to
night, "but I want to improve the tone of the saloons. I consider
the back door of the saloon is the greatest evil that confronts the
city. Before trying to remedy any other abuse I Bhall this mat
ter my careful attention. I refer to the back-door saloons where
women are accommodated. lam told that the number of women in
this city who get drunk every night is appalling, and the evil is
spreading."
The Mayor says that he is giving the saloon question close at
tention, and has called for all the ordinances and statutes bearing
upon the subject in order to prepare a legal recommendation which
he will present to the City Council. The saloon element is much
disturbed over the determined stand taken by Mayor Eaton.
During the municipal campaign the liquor dealers generally sup
ported Eaton, while many of the retail dealers donated liberally to
the fund used by the Republican campaign committee.
Eaton regards the slot machines as an evil that should not be
tolerated. He declares that the percentage is all against the player
and he would rather run against the "Poker" Davis gang and take
his chances than to attempt to come out even with the machines.
Eaton is in intense earnest In regard to these evils with which citi
aeris must contenrW. It woujd not be surprising to those who know
the caliber of the executive were the Council to receive a sensational
communication from the Mayor's pen at its session on Monday next.
He has so far committed himself to this policy of reform that he
has earned the title of the "Doctor Parkhurat of Los Angeles."

cified that two should be voted for. The
facts as regards Creed's sojourn at Ban
Quentin Prison will not be presented
until later.
Richard Garrlty, the Democratic can
didate, received 318 votes; Creed, run
ning independently, 232; and Cramer,
the Republican nominee, 151. If Cramer
succeeds in getting the vote of Sausa
lito Precincts 1 and 2 thrown
out the vote will stand for the remain
ing precinct: Garrity 135, Cramer 120
and Creed 117. Realizing that Cramer
had a good case, Attorney J. W. Coch
rane, representing Creed, asked Judge
Angellotti for leave to amend his an
swer, so that he would be able to prov&
that both Tiburon and Belvedere "did
politics" in the same way, but against
Creed's interests. If they also sye
thrown out the vote will stand: G*.r
rity 72, freed 77 and Cramer 53. He>
was given until next Monday to amend
the complaint.
The case threatens to develop into a
wholesale exposure of the political
methods employed in the southern part
of Marin County, and is exciting a good
deal of interest. An incidental fact
brought out, wHich brought forth ad
verse comment from Judge Angellotti,
was that the official returns on elections
have been thrown carelessly aroured the
County Clerk's o.Tlce. Nooody is blamed
for this, however, as no provision has
ever been made by the county for the
care of the official returns. The envel
ope inclosing the returns from Sausa-
Hto Precinct 1 was not even sealed.

ENGLAND BLOCKS
A RUSSIAN COUP
Naval Demonstration in
Chinese Waters.
WARNING TO CZAR'S AGENTS
-TAD DISPATCHED TBOOPS TO
THE CITY OF PEKING.
Ther Were Hastily Becalled When
tie British Asiatic Squadron
Steamed Into the Gulf
of Pechili.
Special Dispatch to T}r- '".ill.
VICTOR:a, B. C, Jan. 11.â The R. M.
S. Empress of Japan, which left Yoko
hama on December 30, brines a sensa
tional story from Tientsin of how a
naval demonstration by the British
Asiatic fle^t i nder Admiral Sevmeur
caused the ;il>i.ndonment of a coup on
the part of Russia. Had th-i Russian
plans been carr'ed out, they vouM,
without douht, have caused war be
tween the two grtat powers stru^tflin.:
for the mastery in the far East. The
Tientsin correspondent of the North
China Daily Xews, files of which werÂ»!
received by the Empress, has the fol
lowing in this regard:
"A significant bit of information has
just come to me, for the accuracy of
which I am prepared to vouch. At
the time of the demonstration of the
British fleet off Shan Hal Kaun. In the
Gulf of Pechili, thu- Russians were pre
paring to send 5000 troops, mostly Cos
sacks, by forced marches overland
around the Gulf of Liao Tung to the
Chinfse capital.
"When the British squadron steamed
through the straits into the Gulf oÂ£
Pechili from Wei-Hai-Wel in two lim'S.
orders were at once rushed to the 5000
Russians, who had already started for
the Chinese capital, ordering them back
to Port Arthur. The demonstration had
prevented their dispatch. The meaning
of all this is unquestionably this:
"That Russia knew what was prepar
ing in the palace at Peking and was*
sending these troops to the support of
the Empress Dowager. The calling of
the troops back from Shan Shi and
other places in the early part of the
season could not be explained at the
time, but their massing within easy
distance of Peking is now made plain
by the coup d'etat. Possibly had the
Russians reached Peking the native
soldiers would not have been ordered to
the capital, but, in any event, the forces
would have co-operated against any
other foreign force that might have
been sent to Peking in case the status
of those in possession, which meant
Russians, was objected to by any other
power.
"A Peking correspondent interviewed
Sir Claude McDonald, the British Min
ister, at the Chinese capital in this con
nection, and he said that had Russia
dispatch the 5000 troops to Peking that
act would undoubtedly have precipi
tated a clash between Britain and Rus
sia."
"Russia is daily strengthening her po
sition in Chines^ waters," say* thuj-
Pektng and Tientsin Times. "She has
now demanded the inclusion of the
Miao Tao group of islands at the en
trance to the Gulf of Pechili and com
manding the entrance to the gulf in
her Port Arthur sphere of influence.
Great Britain will protest against the
cession of these islands to Russia.
Should Russia push her claim, it is un
derstood, Britain will make a firm
stand, and, according to her Minister
at Peking, fight if necessary."

YOLO COUNTY REPUDIATES BURNS.
YesteTday and the day before Assemblyman O. "W. Pierce of Yolo County took it upon himself
to place D. M. Burns in nomination for United States Senator before the Legislature, and said that it
is the wish of the people of Yolo County that Burns be elected Senator. He declared that Burns had
. never occupied a prison cell in Yolo County, and that the people of that community considered him the
soul of honor and probity. The Call, believing these statements to be untrue and also believing that
they were made for the purpose of intentionally deceiving the membsrs of the Legislature who were
not acquainted with the facts, caused a poll of Winters, one of the principal towns in Yolo County, to
be made. The result is set forth in the following telegram, received last evening:
WINTERS, Jan. 11. â One hundred citizens of this town were interviewed
here today regarding the candidacy of D. M. Burns for United States Sena
tor. The interviews were solicited from prominent citizens, regardless of
their political affiliations. Of the 100, ninety-seven expressed themselves as
being opposed to Burns' candidacy, w hile but three were favorable to it.
The three who are for Burns are Democrats.
A poll was also taken in Davisvllle, Mr. Pierces own home. The result 1b Bet forth in the fol
lowing telegram:
DAVISViLLE, Jan. 11.â A poll of fifty-five prominent Republican citizens
was taken here to-day with reference to their Senatorial preferences. Fol
lowing is the result: Grant 18, Burns 16, Barnes 4, Pardee 4, Bulla 2,
Scott 2, and nine had no particular choice.
It would appear from this that Assemblyman Pierce is either badly mistaken or else he has
small regara for the truth.

BURNS' GREAT POLITICAL COUP
FELL WITH A SICKENING THUD
One Whole Legislator Went Over in a Body and
Voted for Dan.
THE MEXICAN HELD A BOBTAIL FLUSH
An Overwhelming Reserve Force of One Was Thrown Into the Breach and
Then the CandeSaria Miner Found That His Bluff Had Been
Called and He Was Badly Worsted.
CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO, Jan. 11.â The great Burns
coup has been sprung.
The exodus of Grant and Bulla votes
to the bullion-trimmed standard of the
Mexican Senatorial candidate, which
has for two weeks past been adver
tised on the street corners and bawled
out in the barrooms by every heeler
and disreputable tout in the Burns
gang, came to pass this morning dur
ing the calling of the roll in the Assem
bly chamber on the first joint ballot.
Without discordant note of warning
Dan M. Burns threw into his dis
reputable fight the overwhelming re
serve strength that he has vaunted
with all the power of his windy lungs,
and the effect was not demoralizing.
The roll-call of the Senate went
through as it did yesterday, with no
startling developments, and the As
sembly call had progressed to the half
of its length and no inkling of ap
proaching disaster came to darken the
smile of opposing candidates, when
suddenly from the side of the hall, or
from somewhere else, came the signal,
and Assemblyman C. B. Jilson of Sis
kiyou got up and nocked over to the
Mexican forces in a body. Dan
Burns" election for a moment seemed
assured, but the discovery was made
that Jilson could cast but one vote,
and the other candidates resumed their
smiles.
The smiles broadened into grins when
Chenoweth of Orange and Dr. Marvin
of Humboldt, who yesterday put
George Knight in nomination, shouted
"Grant" in lusty voice in answer t"
their names. The loss of Jilson was re
paid and more by the two new votes
and the satisfaction of knowing that
the reward of Jilson's defection would
be the brand of traitor printed deep in
his political hide by all decent people.
Dan Burns' second bluff is called,
and as on yesterday he has been
caught playing the game with a bob
tail flush. The Grant people are in
the same boat, for that matter â the
only difference is in the cards they
held. The forty-five votes that were
to astound the State on the first day
that Burns shied his castor have
dwindled to a paltry twenty-four.
The enormous reserve strength
which, loosed at the right time, was
to carry him with a rush and loud
huzzas from the push into the
United States Senate, has been
sprung on the unsuspecting legisla
tors, and it consists of Assemblyman
Jilson of Siskiyou and Del Norte,
whose acquisition by the Burns
forces dated no further back than
last evening. A second time is
Daniel come to judgment, and twice
he has been found sadly wanting.
Nor has that hegira from Bulla'a
standard to the Grant menage trans
pired. The son-of-his-father has not
yet grown to fit his father's belt, and if
he is to rely upon his present strength
he never will.
ALL OUT IN THE OPEN.
The forces of the leaders in this Sen
atorial battle are all arrayed and out
in the open, where they may be
counted, and unless cash be a more po
tent and abundant factor than it has
already proved it is, out in the open
they bid fair to stay. Caucus is the
hourly cry. but caucus is just now an
enhanced impossibility. The schemes
that have been hatched since that howl
went up have become too well known
to the forces of Bulla and Barnes to
permit or" their falling victims to any
such raw proposition, and the Grant
people have practically given up the
idea and have decided to fight it out on
the floor of the H"use.
To help him in his abortive fight to
day Dan Burns had half his mob of
hired claquers to give off cheers and
rattle the window panes with loud ac
claim at every mention of his name.
They lined the corridors and lobbies,
the office of the sergeant at arms was
crowded with them to suffocation and
they even invaded the floor itself de
spite the injunctions of Speaker
Wright. After the clock had struck the
appointed hour and the Senate was an.
nounoed at the bar of the Assembly j
ready for the joint ballot it required the '
sergeant at arms and his force of as- i
sistants to effect passageway for them
through the crowd of tout? and beer
laden plugs that had mixed with the |
decent element at the prate to the floor. !
A GAY SCENE PRESENTED.
Inside the rail the scene was almost
a gay one. At every desk the wives of [
the members, their friends or those of '
the coming Solons from the Senate had !
found seats, and every available bit i
of standing room was occupied by a
busily chattering crowd of onlookers,

The official doings on the floor were
i not much more than a repetition of
| those of yesterday, with the exception

'â¢That, I imagine," replies Flint, "de

"I move that it be the sense of this

SIMPSON WAS FULL OF TALK.
But Senator Simpson of Orange ob

"I now make a motion that no nomi

He found a second, but Senator Davis

"Mr. President and gentlemen of this

We either are or we are not a deliber

SMITH NOMINATES GRANT.
Senator Smith of Kern then got up
to lend fitting phrase to the nomination
of U. S. Grant. His speech, in effect,
was as follows:
The people of California are most in
tensely interested in this subject. It is
not a matter of our choice; It Is a matter
of performing a great judiciary trust. We
are not here to exercise our individual
likes and dislikes, but to carry forward
the underlying principles of the American
Government, the will of the people, and
he who votes to-day for a candidate
Whom he thinks he has reason to know
would not meet with popular endorse
ment were this matter referred to the
body of the people, strikes a blow at the
perpetuity of American Institutions. The
unusual importance of the selection at
this time is, I think, fully impressed upon
the minds of the members of both
branches of this Legislature. California
occupies a fur greater and more im

PIERCES IDEA OF BURNS.
Mr. Pierce of Yolo turned another trick
for Burns at this point. He said he did
not want to second Burns, he simply :
wanted to tell what the good people of !
Tolo thought of him. He said that Mr. '
Burns' word was the strongest thing in
the rounty. not excepting cheese and Yolo
whisky. Mr. Pierce is 3S years of age; Mr.
Burns about f>4; but Mr. Pierce remem
bered distinctly when they went to school !
together. They called him Captain Dan
then, and Burns did not wear stockings;
he was a "barefooted orphan;" came into
the county that way, in fact, although
he went out wearing patent leathers. Be
recalled his boyhood days at gTeat length
and said that all of the gang now living
was in the lobby at that minute, which
none of his hearers doubted who had
heard the Burns rooters at the railing.
Pierce also denied that "Captain Dan"
had ever robbed Yolo of a cent. On the
contrary, he had come back in his later
years and paid oft the debts he owed
when he left town. Mr. Pierce, however,
made no Mexican references. Continu
ing, he said that Burns was a man in
whom we all have the greatest conildence
and that he had been a potent factor in
all the successes that the Republican
party had attained during the past ten
years; that he had contributed more to
the success of the Republican party than
any other individual in the State of Cal
ifornia, and that he could cope with any
and all questions that might be presented
to him as a member of the United States
Senate.
A GOOD SECOND FOR GRANT.
Senator Cutter of Yuba seconded
Grant's nomination in one of his usual
good speeches. He said:
Opposed by corporate power and cor
porate wealth, arrayed against him the
political machinery of the party, relying
solely upon his own merits and the man
ifest wish and will of the majority of the
people, the name of Ulysses S. Grant has
been presented to this joint assembly for
the office of Senator of the United States.
1 heartily second the nomination. He is
n gentleman of high scholarly attainments
as well as of sound, practical sense. It
is true, Mr. President, that like his dis
tinguished father he Is somewhat reticent
of speech, but there are now
enough members of the United
States Senate who darken wisdom
with multitude of words, and 1 think
It not wise that this joint assembly of
California should add to their number.
We who are his friends and supporters
believe that ultimately he will be the
choice of this joint assembly, and with
confidence and with energy that knows no
defeat we propose to fight it out on this
lino if it takes all winter.

BURNS' MANAGER
CAL.T, HEADQUARTERS. SACRA
MENTO, Jan. 11.â M. M. Estee arrived
from San Francisco this evening. On the
train hither he made the statement to
an old friend and fellow traveler that he
was coming here to manage Dan Burns'
fight for the Senatorship. The change
of management will not prove successful
unless Estee manages Dan's fight with
more skill and sagacity than Dan man
aged Estee' s fight for the same office
several years ago.
The Grant men to-night are deeply
chagrined over the desertion ot Jilson
from their camp. They remark [n ex
planation of the defection that there is
but one step from booze to boodle.
"GUN FIGHTERS" QUIESCENT.
CARSON, Nev., Jan. 11.â All talk of
"gun fights" or personal encounters lia
ble to take place at the Silver State Cen
tral Committee to-morrow should be set
down as the merest bosh. The air is full
Of rumors to the effect that both sides
have Imported "gain fighters" and roughs

They are untrue.

closeted with Stewart and Wallace. A
number of representatives of both houses
arrived to-day and a caucus will be held
Sunday night. It is rumored that the Sil
ver State Central Committee will take a
hand in the Senatorial fight to-morrow,
but it is given out that the call in in re
lation "to the Gubernatorial contest.
METAPHORS THAT WOULD
NOT BE SUPPRESSED
Spellbinders Vigorously Object to Having
Their Oratory Bottled,
Statesmen Spar for an Opportunity to Let Their
Words Ring Unrestrained Through the Long
Suffering Corridors of the Capitol.
BY HENRY JAMES.
CATvL. HEADQUARTERS, BACRA
MRNTO, Jan. 11.â Let it be understood in
tho beginning that I have gone out of tho
prophecy business and have decided to
moke only a plain statement of facts.
To-day occurred the Joint session of
tho two houses. The honors of the occa
sion fell mostly to the Assembly, for the
Senate did most of the talking and, had
nothing Interfered, would have been
talking yet. I think that In the space of
two hours more balderdash never fell
on the human ear. The session early de
veloped the fact that a lot of men wanted
to talk. They sparred for the opportuni
ty and when they had said It marvel de
veloped and grew that they had had the
nerve to hope for the opportunity. A
simple modesty would have prompted
most of them to avoid It as a pestilence.
The Assembly hall was crowded. There
was not a vacant seat and both on the
floor and In the gallery people were stand
ing with a patience almost sublime. Many
ladles were present and perhaps to this
circumstance was due the exuberance of
oratory, for certainly a masculine impulse
to. say "Rats!" must have been recog
j nlzed even by the spell-binders locoed by
the fumes of their mental parts. There
| were pome necessary and rather dreary
i formalities and white these were In prog-
I ress I noticed Sammy Braunhart, now
J serving his last term in any elective posi
! tion. Sammy looked impressive and im
| portant, but remembering the isinglass
story I managed to escape being hypno-
I tized.
Senator Morehmiso tried to shut off the
nominating speeches, raised a storm, and
unhappily met defeat. Simpson arose,
bubbling with enthusiasm or something.
and averred that the time for making
remarks was at hand. He was full of
.them himself. Stratton made the only
really sensible talk of the day, but was
outnumbered. His theory was that the
talk would change no vote, that it would
be a gallery play, largely for the benefit
of reporters; it had the virtue of truth
to back it. Everybody who had ready a
speech roared In protest, but that which
Stratton in his haste had termed a
"Wasteless use" of time seemed to suit the
majority, and a shudder passed over the
throng. As the discussion progressed
Shortridge, who had a seat near the
front, fell into a gentle and becoming
dose. He did not pose, for the Senator
from Santa Clara is free from affecta
tion, but he settled back with the air of
a statesman aweary. At intervals the
clamor of his necktie awoke him, and
then he placed his finger on his lips to
avoid shouting "No" on the motion to
limit nominating speeches.
"Technically speaking," paid Johnson of
Sacramento, "we are not supposed to
know anything as to the speeches made
in separate sessions. I am willing to
waive the technicality and allow the rest
of the proposition to stand."
At last the path to glory was cleared,
and Senator Smith arose to nominate U.
S. Grant. He made a good speech, but it
had no possible effect. It was a flno trib
ute to Grant, utterly devoid of the possi
bility of winning a vote for him.
Inator Wolfe spoke for Burns. Eddie
well.' He is master of a fine heel and
movement, but whether more do
it when standing on his heels or when
â¢ed on the pinnacle of a rigid toe I
am not able to say. He was hanc.icapped
by his subject. He had to extol Burns as
cultured and educated, as a citizen of
standing, as a soldier of renown, and the
fact that Burns has none of these quali
ties and is none of these things might
have rattled a person of less gall. When
he sad Burns was without spot or blem
ish I wondered why Ananias had caught
such a hard biff for fibbing, but when
I hn added that Burns was the choice of
| San Francisco, and the choice of the
j State, which is even now ringing with de
nunciation of him and his ways, Ananias
became a gentleman of comparative ve
j racity. Eddie painted Dan as a reformer,
! a man of character, force and ability,
| with the best citizens for His friends, and
decency staying up nights to pray for his
election. In other words, while the Sena-
I tor did not make an ass of himself, he
in no manner marred his reputation as
one already made. It was a hard task
he had to perform, and that he performed
it without a blush proved him adapted to
a delicate and difficult situation. There
] was applause when he finished. It rame
largely from the gallery, where the claq
uers had secured seats.
Senator Simpson followed. He rambled,
verbose, through the corridors of time
and threatened to infringe upon eternity.
He told about having, at the age of 14,
drawn his sword â probably a tin one scab
barded in his Christmas stocking, but
I this is a mere matter of detail. People
began to get tired and wish he had fallen
on the sword. "I revere Grant," said
Simpson, "and Burns and men of that
class." This strikes me as a new class.
Grant has an apology* due. Yet Simpson
comes from a Prohibition district. Such
cheers as he got were in token of Joy
that he had finished. I almost forgo-t to
say that his object was to nominate Bulla.
for whom he expressed love, and he had
love to spare. In fact, he was in a loving
mood. But Bulla deserved better luck.
, Cosper, Assemblyman, from Kings Coup.-
ty, came to the front and for a time kept
alive a query as - 0 why he had done so.
He Bald Borne k<Â»"1 things, which I will
not quote, for thor- ; was no possible miti
gation for saying thorn. He took up each
candidate In turn, painted him rod, hoist
ed him to the ultimate of a lofty pole and
descanted upon hie unmatched virtues.
Along the Rotten Row of reminiscence ha
trotted astride the lame horse of rhetoric,
while nobody knew why he was there or
at what place he e-r-ected to arrive. All
about him he strewe.l a torrent of
bouquets, having his saddlebags full. Ha
loved the bulging bosom and the musical
voice of Knight, he had a profound ad
miration for the uprightness of Burns,
and therefore he seconded the nomination
of Bulla. I think that with Simpson and
Cosper, Bulla played in hard luck for
the day.
At this Belshaw wanted a limit put on
speeches, but the chairman made only a
fair pretense of hearing him.
Morehouse, who had tried In the first
place to head off nomination epeeches, had
the duty of presenting the name of Gen
eral Barnes. He did it In well chosen
language. The best by far heard during
the session.
Mayor Phelan was offered and got the
full vote of his party when the time came.
Then the seconds began to come in.
They came in fast. Pierce of Yolo was
wound up and the only topic known to
the I'ierce mind is the virtue, worth and
the general integrity of Dan Burns. Ac
cording to Pierce, Dan's Yolo record ia
all right, but he omitted any mention of
the Sacramento record, nor did he say
that on file in the building which echoed
helplessly to his clarion notes were rec
ords showing that Burns owed the State
531,000 for money stolen. He resented the
criticism of the press, not perhaps as the
rogue who begins to feel the halter draw,
but as the frifnd of the rogue. Accord
ing to him. Burns is the best of living
men, and if better there have been his
tory overlooked them. There was an al
most audible sense of relief when Pierce
had run down. Next came the balloting,
of which full accounts are given else- (
where. Victory did not perch on any of
the banners, nor show even a momentary
Impulse to alight. She coyly kept aloof
and the bait to coax her down was not
in evidence. It is not worth while to an
alyze the vote. I would rather devote a
line to the voters.
Kenneally of San Francisco shouted out
"Burns" so loudly as to demonstrate that
he felt proud of the distinction of being
owned by the Candelarian hero, and
L,eavitt of Alameda did the same. Ken
neally has a charge of manslaughter
hanging over him in the city merely be
cause an old man he struck happened
most inopportunely to die, and perhaps a
noise serves to keep the ghost at bay.
Leavitt has not even this sorry pretext to
advance, and I suspect his shout to be the
equivalent of a midnight whistle in a
graveyard.

BERKELEY'S PKOTES*.
BERKELEY. Jan. 11.â The Republicans
of this city were both amused and dis
gusted with the senseless imputations
made against their political sagacity in
the coiumns of this morning's Examiner.
The bald assertion that they were so
many roundheads, fanatically attached to
a narrow sectarianism and dominated by
a blind, crude and relentless antagonism
against any sort of liberal policy, they not
only looked upon as insulting to the last
degree but as utterly ludicrous in its act
ual statement of fact. The university town
besides being a college center is peopled
mostly by business people of San Fran
who make it their place of resi
dence. The Republicans in this district
are composed chiefly of these two classes,
and it is against their intelligence that
the meaningless remarks appearing in the
Examiner were made. They are all united
In saying that from motives of party
policy only their wish was that their rep
resentatlve in the Assembly should vote
against Colonel Dan Burns. They do not
niind being called church people, but they
insist that their political understanding is
quite like that of other people and that
to them Burns' election as United States
Senator wouid be the most unwise move
that the Republicans could be guilty of.
A Call representative called on several
of the leading Republicans here to-day,
and their opinions were given as follows:
Philip R. Boone, principal of Boone's
University Academy and member of the :
Board of Education:
"Yes. I read the effusion on my friend
Wright in the Examiner this morning. I
read it twice. I desired to makÂ« sure
whether the writer was trying to be face
or serious, or a little oft and not
altotgether responsible. I drew the last
conclusion. I was glad to learn, how
ever, that Mr. Wright, when from home,
had sufficient force of character to main
tain his self-respect. Certainly, as 1
know him. he must have felt out of his
sphere when in consultation with the
colonel and his admirers. I know noth
ing of Wright's political relations with
Mr. Burns and the railroad. But I know
mighty well enough to know this, that he
has a mind of his own; that he is true to
his convictions; that he never promised
Mr. Burns his vote; that if he had he
would have voted so. Wright is no
coward, and he cannot be Intimidated.
His constituency must admire him for
his independence whether it be the result
of politics or of 'church' influence. Let
it go at the latter. Berkeley and Oakland
are willing, and I think Wright can
stand it. We shall not lay up anything
against him for this. We congratulate
him that he knew enough to 'keep away
from the devil and out of the deep sea."
May there be many others like him!"
John W. Richards, President of the
Board of Trustees of the city: "I cannot
see that there's any justification in call
ing the people of Berkeley puritanical or
imputing to them any such motives and
inclinations as the article in this morn
ing's Examiner would seem to suggest.
It is even more unjustihable to say that
motives of that kind could have prompted
Howard Wright to vote for Bulia instead
of Burns. A short timo before the Legis
lature convened Mr. Wright came to me
and asked my opinion concerning the sen
timent of the Republicans here about the
Fenatorship. I told him emphatically that
I thought the Republicans here would feel
it a lasting disgrace if thÂ»-lr representative
in the Assembly should vote for Burns. I
told him that his constituency here would
lipoa Burns' election as one of the!
darkest stains upon thf- Republican party
in this State. And this is for reasons of
party policy merely. It is because we
reel that Burns is altogether unworthy of
the high office which he seeks, and that
such a man would rum the policy and
prevent the future success of the party
that we have asked Mr. Wright to vote
against him, and not from any such *ense
less motives as was claimed."
Professor Carl C. Plehn of the depart
ment of history and political economy
of the University of California, and one
of the leading Republicans of this city:
"I think that such a characterization of
the people of Berkeley should oe taken
as a compliment. Such people have the
best Interests of the party at heart, and
they would shrink from seeing its polio
twisted into securing political plums for
unworthy men."
Guy Hyde Chick, City Superintendent of

sufficiently strong to not only change
Wolfe's vote, but also to induce him
to place Burns in nomination before

Judge Dibble votes for Burns in a
clear tone of voice and he is ready at
any time to engineer a caucus for the
colonel, but the oratorical voice of
Dibble had not been heard in behalf of
Burns, and therefore lamentations may
be heard where the touts congregate.
Assemblyman Clark of Santa Clara
was told directly that he could not ex
pect to pass a bill or do anything in
the Legislature for his constituents if
he did not at once get into line for
Burns. The Assemblyman signified
that he had only one important meas
ure to pass, but if necessary' n^ would
sacrifice his prospects of its success
rather than vote against the will of the
people who sent him to the Legislature.
He reasoned that he had only sixty
days to live in Sacramento, whereas he
expected to pass the rest of his life in
Santa Clara County. He preferred,
therefore, to so conduct himself in the
Legislature that he could go home to
his friends and neighbors and continue
to enjoy their confidence and esteem.
The readiness of the Grant men to
take a second ballot in joint assembly
to-day seemed to outsiders as an indi
cation of confidence upon the part of
Grant's managers. It was a bluff
rather than a display of confidence. It
was known In advance that Burns was
not willing to go ahead on another bal
lot, because his touts failed to con
nect last night on several important
engagements. Burns calculated on ad
ding four votes to his black list, but
those who were staked out to be listed
withheld consent. Green and Brown,
the Grant colors, then came to the
front, challenging another test of
speed.
The Burns oratory to-day was dis
piriting to the touts. "Wolfe was lame
and Pierce was silly. The Senators
and Assemblymen who have consented
to stand for Burns were not willing to
go ahead with another ballot. Some of
them cherish the hope that they will
be released from the "peculiar obliga
tion" to support Dan before the dawn
of another day.
There is no doubt to-night that Judge
Dibble, Grove Johnson and several
others of the Legislature would like to
have a close heart to heart talk with
Herrln to convince him of the" utter
folly of prolonging the contest on be
half of Dan Burns. They do not say
so. but actions sometimes speak louder
than words.

resolution was promptly strangled, which
action is deserving of flattering comment.

for that pa: .

One of the rawest little jobs that is

Simpson's resolution providing for the

Smith of Kern declared that for his part

Then Simpson insisted upon talking

on <inu c.i. The resolution was killed by

NO MORE JILSONS
IN THE GRANT CAMP
CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO, Cal.. Jan. 11.â The twenty-seven
Senators and Assemblymen who voted for
U. S. Grant on joint ballot to-day will
vote for him again to-morrow. This is
what they decided to do In caucus to
night. There are no more Jilsons In the
Grant camp just now, and Governor Gage
and the State patronage club cannot be
used for the present to drive any more
of Grant's supporters into the Burns
camp.
All but three of the twenty-seven Grant
men were present at caucus this even-
Ing. Assemblyman Crowder of San Diego
County was sick. Assembiyman Cheyno
with of Orange County was detained on
account of sickness in his family. As
semblyman Merrill was not* there, but he
sent word that he would be bound by the
action of the caucus.
The whole situation was thoroughly dis
cussed by the twenty-four who were pres
ent. Not one of them seemed to be at all
disheartened at the situation as it exists.
After some considerable talk a resolution
was offered and unanimously adopted that
those participating in the conference
wouid continue to vote for Mr. Grant un
til another caucus was called. If at any
time any of the present supporters ot
Grant desire to be released m order to
go to the support of some other candidate
notice is to be given, a caucus will be
called and the legislator desiring to leave
Grant will be released. Mr. Grant ad
dressed the caucus, briefly expressing
himself as highly satisfied with the re
sult of the first two ballots. He thanked
his friends for the support they had given
him. and added that he did not propose
to ask any one to vote for him any longer
than he thought there was a prospect for
him to be elected. If the time ever ar
rived when it was deemed advisable to
withdraw his name. Mr. Grant stated that
he was perfectly waling to step aside and
permit any gentleman they might select
to take his place.
Mr. Grant's remarks were received with
satisfaction by his supporters, and they
all reaffirmed their loyalty to him and
their intention to remain true to his
standard.
The caucus decided to make an effort
to secure an expression of some kind from
the Republicans of Siskiyou county as to
whether or not the action of Assembly
man Jilson is deserting Grant and going
over to the support of Burns met with
their approval.
The caucus then adjourned, to meet
again on the call of any of Mr. Grant's
supporters. His managers were very hap
py at the results accomplished by the
conference, for it was an assurance to
tnem that their strength is not to be
broken for a while at least, and any ad
ditional votes they get to-morrow will
be a net gain. There will be no off
sets by deserting.
Clara Barton tells about the work
of the Red Cross in Cuba in next
Sunday's CalL

for Burns.
All the Influence of the Execative Is at the Disposal

HaVe Little Effect,

j which will cover the State of California i
! from his home county of Siskiyou, on the !

j county of San Diego, where Ulysses S. !
i Grant Jr. resides, will Assemblyman Jil- i
I son be known from this time on. Here j

| quarters of this same U. S. Grant, Mr.
Jilson is already known.
Until yesterday Mr. Jilson was highly
thought of by all the lieutenants, cor
â  porals and privates who are handling the

supposed to be one of Mr. Grant's most
i staunch supporters, and was regarded as

pare one of the seconding speeches to be i

lie through the San Francisco papers a

I Assemblyman from far off Siskiyou who I

; yesterday afternoon. The various nomi- i
; nating and seconding speeches were made ;

quence.
Then the Grant men got frightened. !
: Anxiously they waited for the call of the

clerk called the alphabetically arranged
I list of Assemblymen they held their ;
I breath until he "ot to the letter "J."
; Then the name of Jilson was called and <
! he voted for Grant.
This eased the minds of Milton Green, !
; James Brown and United States Marshal '
i Shine. After all, perhaps there was no i
I ground for their suspicion that the As-
I semblyman had fallen by the roadside in !
the ardor of his support for Grant's can- j
: didacy.
But to-day, in the Joint session. Assem- j
blyman Jilson gave the Grant managers !
I a shock from which they have not yet j
recovered. When his name was reached
: on the Assembly rollcall he cast his vote 'â 
for Daniel M. Burns. Then- it was that
the Grant people realized their suspicions
, of the day previous as to the loyalty oi ,
'. Jillson were well founded. Nevertheless,
; they were surprised beyond measure, and
after the joint session had adjourned they i
1 sent out an army of detectives to ascer
: â  tain the reasons, monetary or otherwise,
i that Assemblyman Jilson had for chang- j
â  ing his vote from Grant to Burns.
The one man who is responsible for the ;
i change of the vote of Assemblyman Jilson i
'â  to-day is Hervey Lindley, the ex-Repub- )
1 lican boss of Los Angeles County, who :
! has a large log-rolling plant in Siskiyou \
! County but who is just at present engaged
I rolling logs in Sacramento to secure' the
, election of the man from Mexico. Lind-
I ley is one of the most shrewd political !
wire-pullers in the State, and at no time
â  since his arrival in Sacramento have the :
Grant people had the vote of Assembly
man Jilson.
Lindley called in to assist him in win
ning over the vote of Jilson to Burns Dr.
C. L. Gregory, the chairman of the Re
publican committee of Siskiyou County.
The doctor arrived in the city Monday,
and ever since then he has been working |
with his friend, Assemblyman Jilson, to
! secure his vote for burns. 10-day ue ac
j complished his purpose, and Hervey Lind
i ley once more demonstrated that he has
not forgotten that political cunning and
trickery for which he is noted.
It was the State patronage and the fact
I that, if Jilson continued to vote for Grant
neither he nor the Republicans of Siski
-1 you County would receive any of it. that
, was a powerful factor in the change of
his vote to Burns.
To the Grant people Mr. Jilson gave
: his reason for changing his vote. He told
' them that Dr. Gregory, the chairman
j of the county committee had come to
i him and stated that the Republicans of
I Siskiyou County would approve of his
j vote for Burns, and that if he did so the
I Republicans of that county > would stand
some show of securing recognition in the !
I division of State patronage. But if he

age is at the disposal of Mexican

Dr. C. L. Gregory was seen this evening
and admitted that he had made the state
ments accredited to him by Assemblyman
Jilson. He took all the responsibility that
the remarks he had made to Jilson car
rled with them. He said that he had good !
and sufficient reasons for stating that it
Jilson continued to vote for Grant. Siski
you County Republicans would never for
a moment be considered by Governor
Gage in passing out State jobs. This. Dr.
Gregory said, he did not like, and he
therefore thought it wise for Jilson to
come over to the support of Burns.
Dr. Gregory was very frank to admit

Scott had many admirers among the Re- ,
publicans of Siskiyou County. He said

licans that Burns should be elected Sen
ator. His opinion was that they would

that has been used to whip Assemblyman
Jilson into the Burns column.
The hand of the- Governor of California
is apparent in this little deal, and it is

cover it. The Grant men have not alto
gether given up hope as far as the vote
of Assemblyman Jilson is concerned. He
has told them that in a day or two he

for' their candidate. They do not, how

ise. He has fooled them once and they I

to it that those who are liable to b* sus
ceptlble to the influences that might be i

Those who are regarded as somewhat ]
weak in their support of Grant are being

attempt made to reach them will be re
ported at Grant headquarters, and then
the Grant Assemblyman or Senator who
is being tampered with will be placed on
his guard.

THE BLACK LIST GROWS.
WJ HEN The Call published yesterday morning
a'" list of the men who had so far forgotten
themselves as to vote for Dan Burns for
United States Senator, there was reason to suppose
the black array complete.
Nobody dreamed that any other member of cither
House would so far forget his honor, his duty to his
Constituents and to society as to voluntarily sign the
roll of dishonor. Yet Jilson of Shasta has done this.
He has bartered his reputation for a vain hope, and
has joined the forces of Burns. There is not much
to say. The only necessity is to publish the black
list again, adding to it this new name. Here is the
liÂ«t as it now appears:
SENATORS. -
Bettman, San Francisco.
Burnett, San Francisco.
Hoey, San Francisco.
Laird, Shasta.
Leavitt, Alameda.
Shortridge, Santa Clara.
Wolfe, San Francisco.
ASSEMBLYMEN.
Arnerich, Santa Clara.
Barry, San Francisco.
Beecher, Shasta.
Cobb, San Francisco.
Devoto, San Francisco.
Dibble, San Francisco.
Henry, San Francisco.
JILSON, Siskiyou.
Johnson, Sacramento.
Kelley, Alameda.
Kelsey, Santa Clara.
Kenneally, San Francisco.
Lundquist, San Francisco.
McKeen, Alameda.
Miller, San Francisco.
Pierce, Yolo.
Rickard, San Francisco.
Eugene Sullivan, San Francisco.
There is no pleasure in adding to the catalogue of
political infamy. What may have been the induce
ment to Jilson tt) annex himself to the number al
ready branded matters not. The fact appears that he
has done so, and no explanation will in any measure
shield him. He has defied the warnings of the peo
ple of the State. He has cast his lot with the des
;/cradoes of politics. He can expect no forgiveness,
and he has cut himself off from the possibility of
favors.
Perhaps the list will grow, but space is ample.
Every man who joins the Burns forces shall have ad
vertising free. It has been said from the beginning
that whoever switched to the tenderloin gang after
the first vote would be regarded as worse than the
original int. Jilson is first. Xe\t!
STILL DRIVING NOILS.
THE Examiner is in a bad way. It has ostcn
tattously ceased to publish the news. On Tues
day it omitted Senator Hoar's great speech.
Yesterday it catered to domestic taste in two pages
on Sharkey'i victory, and declared editorially that it
was "first not only in news, but in fiction," from the
last clauÂ«r o! which assertion there will be no dis
sent. It failed, however, to print any portion of Sen
;uor Mason's splendid argument against imperialism,
which, even in the Senate Chamber, excited the ap
plause of spectators and received the congratulations
of thr Senators. By this time the world has read the
substance of that argument, but not in the columns
of the Examiner. Young Mr. Hearst is evidently
very busy driving nails, which he supposes to be pass-
ing through the American flag, but which in reality
ar<:tightly closing up 1m own journalistic coffin.
The Examiner still continues to call attention to
the claim of being the only paper in a position to
#ive all the Sacramento news, but neglects to back
this up by giving the news.
There ought to be an understanding with the next
Arctic explorer that the relief business had been
flayed out. He ought to provide himself with a self
rescuing apparatus.
Â. According to a dispatch Mrs. Charlotte Smith is
again on the warpath in behalf of the "sex to which
she. belongs." Which sex is that?
If Grant is to be beaten, a regrettable feature of the
case Kill be the chance it will give Gaee to crow.
GAGE PERSONALLY AFFRONTED.
THE new Governor of the State may not realize
that he has placed himself in a position which to
any other man would be embarrassing. He had
declared that a vote cast for Grant for Senator would
be regarded by him as a personal affront. It was not
a proper thing for a Governor to say, and there was
at first a doubt, but Gage reiterated the statement, nor
seemed to know that he had violated any canon of
good taste.
Now the Governor has been affronted. He has
! been affronted by twenty-eight Republicans. They
i have dared to vote for the man for whom, with an ar
! rogance unparalleled, he had forbidden them to cast a
] ballot. True, Jilson recanted and went over to Gage's
! man Burn?, but the executive will had been defied,
j the executive temper ruffled, and whether Jilson will
1 be able to find his way into the sunshine of executive
I approval remains to be decided. The value of bask
| ing there is a problem, anyhow. If to secure the
benediction of the Governor one must be a Burns
man, and receive the condemnation of the honest peo-
pie of the State, the happiness can hardly be worth
the price.
Twenty-eight personal affronts constitute a con
siderable dose. As an antidote is the comforting fact
that twenty-five members of the Senate and Assembly
have indorsed Burns, the man who is backed by
every corrupt influence of the State, from the gam
blers, headed by Gunst, down to the railroad com
pany, which draws its inspiration from Huntington
through the instrumentality of Herrin. He has on
his side the telephone company, every swinish cor
poration, every organized enemy of the people. Let
him be consoled. Those who fear the wrath of the
Governor are naturally trembling. Those who re
gard his blows as the taps from a distended bladder
are watching with interest to observe the fall of his
vengeance. Can it reach beyond the haughty stare
and the portentous frown? Are honest legislators to
be swept from public life by a scowl or chased into
seclusion by the hostile squeak of the gubernatorial
boot?
Because the Governor entertained a distaste for one
member of the Associated Press he had caused every
paper in California great inconvenience. It is not im-
J proper to remark that this distaste was mutual and
that there is a cheering prospect of its remaining so.
Nevertheless the retaliation was too comprehensive
to be fair; it was on the Chinese system of punish
ing not alone the offender, but all his relatives and
associates. If he carry the same scheme into effect
j among the legislators none will escape. He will de
vastate both houses as a malady, and the tombstones
erected over political ambitions will gleam white and
terrible under the searchlights of history, a warning
to the indiscreet statesman not to monkey with the
I buzzsaw, a silent and lasting testimonial to the awful
ness of the implacable wrath of a Governor in war
paint.
Gage has twenty-eight marked for slaughter, but it
is to be hoped that he will spare the twenty-five inno
cent lambkins gamboling and bleating at his heels,
himself their guiding star, and Burns the gentle
shepherd with more than one crook.
THE SOLANO MURDER MYSTERY.
WHILE our contemporaries are seemingly in
capable of doing more than report the Sena
torial contest at Sacramento or prize-fights in
New York, The Call, without neglecting either of
these things, and while giving them all the space
their news value requires, carries on extensive work
in other fields and gathers for its readers all the news
of the day.
A striking illustration of this was shown yesterday
when The Call scored an important scoop by giving
a full account' of the arrest of William J. Miller,
charged with the murder of Daniel H. Wilson, an
aged rancher of Solano County, whose tragic death
constituted one of the most mysterious crimes that
have puzzled detectives. With the report of the ar
rest was given a full account of the crime, together
with the testimony which directs suspicion toward
the arrested man.
The crime was committed in October, 1896, and for
two years the authorities worked in vain to discover
the assassin. Step by step, however, the trail was fol
lowed, and one by one facts were discovered which
when taken together form a strong chain of evidence
against Miller. The Call, in its wide search for news,
learned of this evidence, and was therefore prepared
to give the whole story to the public as soon as the
climax was reached by the arrest of the suspected
man.
The story was unquestionably the most important
Californian news item of the day. It throws Hght
upon one of the most mysterious crimes of recent
years and discloses facts which promise to bring to
justice the perpetrators of the deed. Its publication
by The Call, while no other morning paper had it,
is another of the many illustrations given of late of the
superiority of The Call's news service over that of all
of its competitors.
THE IMPORTANCE OF FORESTS.
BY the appointment of a committee to urge upon
the faculties of the State University and of
Stanford the establishment of a chair of for
estry in each of these institutions^the State Board of
Trade has taken action upon a subject of vast and in
creasing importance to the people of California.
The time has conic for us to do something for the
protection of our forests, and one of the important
steps in that direction is to provide for scientific edu
cation on the subject. A liberal provision has been
made for such study in New York at Cornell Uni
versity, and a large area of forest land has been placed
at the disposal of the faculty so that the experiments
may be carried out upon an extensive scale. Califor
nia has an even greater need than New York to pro
tect her forests, and ought, therefore, to be at least
equally vigilant in preparing to do so.
In the current number of the Atlantic Monthly
President Eliot of Harvard, in an article on the "De
structive and Constructive Energies of our Govern
ment." after pointing out how much more money is
expended for war purposes than for the development,
improvement and protection of our industrial re
sources, makes a strong plea for a comprehensive
system of forest preservation, and notes that the ex
perience of other nations has already demonstrated
that well managed national forest reserves not only
pay their expenses but yield a revenue.
"Any one," says President Eliot, "who has trav
eled through the comparatively treeless countries
around the Mediterranean, such as Spain, Sicily,
Northern Africa and large portions of Italy, must
fervently pray that our own country may be pre
served from so dismal a fate. It is not the loss of the
forests only that is to be dreaded, but the loss of
agricultural regions now fertile and populous, which
may be desolated by the floods that rush down from
bare hills and mountains, bringing with them vast
quantities of sand and gravel to be spread over the
lowlands."
The experience of all lands shows that the protec
tion of forests is one of the most important charges
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, JANVAIIV 12, 1899.
j resting upon the governments of civilized people. In
j our own country very little has been done either by
the nation or by States to fulfill the duty this respon
sibility imposes. We have not even protected" the
forests from wasteful fires, though, as President Eliot
says, "the experience of Canada has proved, under
conditions analogous to those which exist in our own
territory, that forest guards and patrols can do much
tc keep down fires, even in the driest seasons."
In the meantime, while the importance of forest
protection is being urged upon the States and upon
the nation, it will be well for our universities to be
gin to give scientific education upon the subject.
Sooner or later the work of forestry will have to be
undertaken on a large scale, and there will then be
needed men who understand it. When that time
comes it should not be necessary for us to have to
send abroad for experts to take charge of the work,
', We should have the men at home. Berkeley and Stan
ford, therefore should begin the training as promptly
as possible.
THE members of the Legislature who are sup
porting Burns for Senator are perhaps in that
hardened condition that makes them insensible
to the greatness of the trust which their action be
trays.
It is the practice of some of them to say that Burns
must be "vindicated" against the abuse of the press.
This is idle talk. It would be far better to point out
anything untrue that is said by the press. Every word
published in The Call about Burns is taken directly
from the public records. His default as County Clerk
of Yolo County and as Secretary of State is a matter
of public and judicial record. His arrest and convic
tion of crime in Mexico are of judicial record there.
Who will arise in the Legislature to deny these j
records or explain them away?
The legislators who feel no aversion for such a
record, and who propose to send to the United States
Senate the man who made it, are assuming the gravest
lof responsibilities. The safety of the public funds,
the money of the people, depends upon the honor
and honesty of a large number of fiduciary officers.
When the duplex infidelity of Burns to a like trust is
1 condoned by sending him to the Senate, the safety
of every dollar in public custody is imperiled. If the
people of California treat the embezzlement of public
funds as so light a thing as not to bar the officer re
sponsible for it from election to the United States
Senate, any latent germ of dishonesty in every such
officer is quickened into life. The honest man in an
office of financial trust feels that the line between him
and the dishonest man has not merely been sponged
out, but that whatever high reward is in prospect is
easier within the dishonest man's reach. The guar
dians of public morality feel keenly the conseqtfences
of such action as the support of Burns. They feel
; that it is a distinct discouragement of personal and
| official honor and honesty, and encouragement of de
[ falcation, embezzlement and theft in public station.
It is not abuse of a candidate for the Senatorship !
to publish his record. But it is abuse of the legisla- ;
tive office to brazenly support a man whose record j
when so published reveals a career of official and
business dishonesty. Will the list of such legislative \
betrayers of a trust grow longer or shorter as the bal- j
lcting progresses? Will the ranks of those who pro- '
pose to glorify the embezzlement of public money and
approve the rank betrayal of official trust increase or j
diminish? Will those who propose to put the Repub
lican party in a position which it cannot defend, I
and which will defeat it until the foul act is forgotten, j
stand by the position they have taken, or will they
save the honor of their State, the credit of their party !
and their own names from infamy by retreating while j
there is yet time?
EVASIVE BEEF CONTRACTORS.
AS was to have been expected, the testimony of
army officers concerning the vile nature of the
refrigerated beef furnished to the army during
the Cuban campaign has roused all the great meat
-1 packing houses of Chicago, and they are now busy in
| putting before the country a vast amount of evidence
i tending to prove that their meat is altogether whole
! some, and that even when it looks bad it is really
good.
One of these gentlemen, in referring to the rej,ort
of the army inspectors that much of the meat fur
nished the troops had "a green beard" on it, said that
the wholesalers who supplied the best hotels in Chi
; cago received an advance price for "aged" beef after
it had accumulated the "beard," and that beef was
better in every respect after being aged. The state
ment will no doubt be something of a surprise to the
j patrons of the Chicago hotels, and is calculated to
I increase the consumption of bacon and other forms
, of meat that do not grow a beard before being served.
It is further asserted that the Secretary of Agricul-
I ture, in order to relieve the strain on the trade, pro
, poses to make an investigation of various brands of
refrigerated beef sold in the American market or pre
pared for export. The object of his investigation is
to prevent as far as possible any injury to the meat
trade, honestly conducted, by exposing that which is
bad.
That some action of the kind is necessary is not to
be denied, for already the disclosures made concern
j ing the "embalmed beef" have seriously affected the
demand for refrigerated beef, and officials connected
with the Department of Agriculture are quoted as
saying: "The attacks made on the beef, even if not
sustained, will cost the meat interests of the country
an amount equal to the entire war. and furnish ma
terial in the future for the opposition of those coun
tries which have been all along fighting the admission
of American beef products."
All of these investigations tending to relieve our
! beef trade of suspicion are excellent in their way, and
will have the approval of all classes of the American
people. It must be borne in mind, however, that they
do not affect the issue raised by the reports from the
army. It has not been stated that all refrigerated
beef is bad, but that the beef furnished for the troops
wzs bad, and that issue is not to be met by evidence
showing that Chicago hotels prefer to have beef with
?. beard on it, nor by further evidence that as a rule
the beef is good.
The testimony of General Miles and his officers is
! th;it the contractors supplied the troops at Tampa
inri at Santiago with beef that smelled like an em
balmed body, that it tasted bitter and foul, that when
boiling it emitted a sickening odor, that the troops
could not eat it, and that large quantities of it were
dumped into the sea because it was worse than no
food at all.
That outrage against the army is one the American
people will not condone merely for the sake of hush
ing up a scandal against the beef trade. We are a
commercial people, it is true, but we are also a pa
triotic people, and are not going to permit a white
wash of contractors who in their greed and reckless
ness were willing to poison hundreds of soldiers by
supplying them with vile beef when these same con
tractors were being paid high prices by the Govern
ment for good meat.
All that the people of this country can learn about
Dreyfus is that they cannot learn anything about him.
c TRUST BETRAYED.
IMPROVEMENT
IN THE WORLD
OF REALTY
Records Show an
Advancement.
MANY SALES ARE NEGOTIATED
ADVENT OF RAIN BRINGS UNI
VERSAL ENCOURAGEMENT.
A Glance at the Records, New Build
ing Contracts, Recent Sales
and General Random.
Notes.
Realty during the past week has shown
perceptible signs of improvement. Sales,
although not unusually large, are becom
ing more numerous and are showing a
tendency toward greater values.
Probably the greatest encouragement
that could possibly come to both city and
country property-owners is the recent
rainstorm, it has revivified the crops,
which means a prosperous year to farm
ers and the ultimate payment of mort
gages. Although the real estate Interests
in the city will not experience, to the
same extent, the beneficial results, still
it cannot possibly help showing an im
provement.
According to the monthly real estate
circular of Thomas Magee & Sons the
total number of mortgages for this city
during the year of 1898 was 2602, with an
aggregate value of $10,154,754. Th,e savings
banks, according to the same authority,
loaned $$,325,281 on city real estate during
the year and released mortgages thereon
amounting to $6,229,618; the net amount
loaned by them over the releases was
therefore only $2,090,663, or, if what the
three banks loaned is counted, it amounts
to about $40,000 each per month only of
new money.
As Quoted from the circular the number
and value of the real estate sales made in
the city and county of San Francisco in
the year 1898 was only 2646 with a total
value of $10,747,104, against 3215 sales of the
value of $12,993,025 in 1897.

MERCHANTS DENOUNCE
EXAMINER METHODS
SCORCHING RESOLUTIONS BY THE
GROCERS' ASSOCIATION.
The Yellow Sheet's Position on a
Certain Bill Scored as Dis
honest and Contrary
to Public Welfare.
Long and emphatic were the denuncia
tions of "Examiner methods" indulged in
by the members of the Retail Grocers' j

last night at B'nai B'rlth Hall. Over a ;
hundred of the leading grocers of the city j

plaint at the way in which the Examiner
had lied about the purport of the bill the i
Grocers' Association, together with thu
Butchers' Board of Trade, intends to pre
sent to the present Legislature.
The general opinion, as expressed by
many members, seemed to be that the
Examiner was seeking to gain a little ;
fake prestige by working on the sympa
tblea of 111-informed people with false |
statements as to what the merchants
wished.

mously adopted:

Butchers' Board ol Trade are working tor tin
passage of a bill to revive the infamous pr&c
ttce of Imprisonment for debt; and, whereas,

interest, we would be the first to oppose a
measure that would tend to brand a man as a

take away the wage-earning capacity of the
poor man; and whereas, a man unable to pay
hiÃ bills through misfortune or otherwise
could not be imprisoned anri would not be af
fected in the least by our bill; and whereas,
the bill we are urging would only tend to >-n
force payment from thos<- who can pay but
will not, and is now working to great ad
vantaee in the State of Massachusetts; ami
whereas, the Examiner, by its false and will
ful mtPÃtatements of what we ask in our bill
has inflamed the mlmls of many unthinking
and 111-informed people with the result that
our labor In securing the passage of this bill
will be made greater; therefore, he it
Resolved, That the Retail Grocers' Associa
tion condemns the position of the Examiner as
being:
Firstâ Dishonest, in that it misstates the
true purpose of the law.

Resolved, That the retail merchants all oter

from this sensational paper which, to

it further
Resolved, That while we favor advertising,
we protest against the methods of this malo
dorous example of the "new journalism,"
which seeks to gain some little advantage for
itself by sacrificing the welfare of the mer
chants of the entire Stn.te.
