STANFORD PARLOR
EVOLVES A NEW IDEA
JOHN J. LERMEN AT A BANQUET
TALKS OF THE FUTURE.
Pleasant Gathering of Natives That
Followed the Installation
of Officers.
Stanford Parlor No. 76, Native Sons of
the Golden West, has evolved a new idea j
with a view to bring itself and the other
Native Sons into deserved prominence, j
and that idea was publicly announced j
last Tuesday night at a banquet given j
after the Installation of the following j
officers for the present term by District i
Deputy Grand President L. W. Cobble- |
dick, assisted by Henry Lansing, D. D. G.
P., as marshal: John J. McCarthy, past
president; D. A. Curtin, president; Will
iam A. Deane, first vice president; Will
iaqi 11. Dick, second vice president; Will- !
iam F. Humphreys, third vice president; ;
Fred H. Jung, recording- secretary: Joseph j
G. Cox, financial secretary; Frank Ecken
roth, treasurer; William D. Hynes, mar
shal; E. H. Hammer, outside sentinel; T.
F. Noonan. outside sentinel; Judge Frank
H. Kerrigan. J. J. Lermen and A. F.
Schleicher, trustees; Dr. A. H. Glannini,
Dr. A. R. Bryant, Dr. Charles Clark. Dr.
H. Kugler, Dr. Alfred McLaughlin and
Dr. Fred B. Sutherland, surgeons.
At the banquet there were present about
150, Including several specially invited
guests. Dr. A. H. Gianninl was introduced I
by President Curtin as toastmaster, and
after a few well-chosen words by way of
introduction proceeded at once to call
upon those who had been selected to re
spond to sentiments, giving the members
and guests an opportunity to partake of 1
the good things laid before them and at i
the same time listen to oratory, for within ]
its membership Stanford Parlor has a
number of "natural born speakers. "
Occupying seats of honor near the I
toastmaster were Sheriff H. S. Martin ,
grand treasurer of the order and a prom
inent member of the parlor, physically
and fraternally; Grand Secretary Lun
fttedt, Judge J. E. Barry, John J. Lermen
secretary of the Yosemite Commission-
President Curtin. District Deputy Cobble- ;
dick, Past President McCarthy, District
Deputy Hy Lansing, Past President J. j
W. Lewis, C. H. Stanyan and Dr. W. H !
Seiberst.
After an overture of popular airs by the
orchestra W. W. Finney favored with
vocal selections, winding up with "Just
One Girl," all present joining in the
chorus; Past President McCarthy re
sponded to "Our Coming Celebration '
and called attention to the fact that the.
members of the parlor had subscribed to
a fund to make a first-class showing dur
ing the celebration in 1900; that the mem
bers were paying their subscriptions in
installments, and that he felt sure that
Stanford Parlor would be a shining mark
in the grandest demonstration ever
planned in California. A quartet composed
of \V. J. O'Brien. C. Hanley, Ben Tarbox
and F. K. Thompson rendered Beveral
choice selections, after which Mr. Lermen
explained "The Future of Stanfojd Par
lor."
It was in substance that the parlor has
appointed a committee consisting of thf>
speaker as chairman, Rudolph Herold and
Henry 8. Martin to arrange for the re
ception in the future of persons promi
nent in art, science and literature by the
parlor so as to give them an idea of what
the Native Sons are as an organization,
and in that way place the parlor on a
high plane and at the same time extend
the fame of the order beyond the con
fines of the State. The speaker said that
Leland Stanford, from whom the parlor
took its name, had, by his interest in the
cause of education, established a world
wide fame and that there was no reason
why the parlor in branching out into a
high field should not become famous.
Then followed responses to toasts by
District Deputy Cobbledlck, Henry S.
Martin, Judge Barry. Grand Secretary
Lunstedt and W. J. Demartinl. the pres
entation of a handsome diamond-set
badge to retiring Past President Lewis,
a clever skit in verse by W. D. Hynts,
who supplemented with stories, and a
bright quarter of an hour with Judge
Kerrigan, who, railed upon to respond to
"The Ladifs." told many witty things,
but never nnce referred to the toast sug
gested by the toast master.
GOOD NEWS FROM
THE PLAYHOUSES
The coming week promises a great deal
in the way of comic opera attractions. At
the Columbia our old friend, Frank Dan
iels, whom we have yet to greet as a
comic opera star, opens a season, during
which he will produce "The Idol's Eye"
and "The Wizard of the Nile," both com
posed by Victor Herbert and both East
ern successes of the first rank. Not to be
behind hand, the Tivoli will open the
new week with "The Wedding Day," a
comic opera by Julian Edwards, com
poser of "Brian Boru," that was played
for two seasons in the East by the
Lillian Russell-Delia Fox-Jeff de Angells
Company. In the meantime Willie Col
lier in "The Man from Mexico" Is crowl
ing the Columbia, and "The Yellow
Dwarf" is furnishing a good run at the
Tivoli.
One of the best of the American plays
of local color is "Pudd'n-Head Wilson."
which is receiving a careful production in
the California at tin; hands of Edwin
Muyo and his excellent company. The
original "Yon Yonson" comes next week.
"The Magistrate" is doing well at the
Alcazar, and will give way Monday to
the long-promised production of "A Tem
perance Town."
The Comedy Theater Is offering "Uncle
Tom's Cabin." with the John F. Stowe
Company. Gottlob and Marx have the
house next week and offer "Tennessee's
Pardner," an attraction originally billed
for the Calif ornin, but now unavoidably
side-tracked on account of the Baldwin
fire.
The Hi Henry Minstrels finish a fort
night's run with the Sunday night per
formance, after which Morosco's closes i
for repairs.
Paplnta, in her dazzling dances, and
Barton Hill and other legitimate players
in a one-act farce are the stellar features
of a good Orpheum bill.
Big Judgment Entered.
A default judgment was ordered en
tered by Judge Belcher In favor of the
Hobart Estate Company and against
Evan Williams for $25,000 and interest,
being on a note issued on June 14, 1895.

in camp at Chickamauga and from what
he has to say it is evident that the men
at Chickam&uga were subject to the same
trials that aroused so much comment re
garding Camp Merritt.

PRELIMINARY EXAMINATION
COMMENCED.
Delia Richardson Tells of Angry
Words Between Flynn and
Gallagher Prior to the
Shooting.
The difficulty In the murder case of John
Flynn, saloon-keeper, was overcome yes
â¢ morning by Policeman D. E. Bax
ter swearing to a complaint before Judge
m charging Flynn with the crime.
Baxter was the officer who arrested Flynn
in his saloon shortly after the shooting,
and, besides, the shooting took place on
his beat.
Flynn was formally booked on thÂ«
charge, and appeared for his preliminary
examination yesterday afternoon. He
was arraigned, and J. X. E. Wilson an
nounced that he represented him. Prose
cuting Attorney Wentworth conducted the
ution. The courtroom was crowded.
Dr. Zobt'i of the Receiving Hospital
Bed to James Gallagher being brought
to the hospital on New Year's morning
suffering from a gunshot wound, from
which he died. He did not probe for the
bullet, and consequently did not know its
course.

the star witness, testified to being in Sul

VOGELSANG
A BENEDICT
A Quiet Ceremony at Grace
Church â Cinder el las
Meet.
Alexander Vogelsang, attorney and Fish |
and Game Commissioner, was married j
yesterday afternoon at 4 o'clock to Miss
Fannie Johnson, daughter of the late J.
M. Johnson. It was a very quiet wed
ding, and only the immediate relatives
assembled in Grace Church to witness the
nuptial ceremony, which was performed j
by Rev. Dr. Foute. Fred S. Johnson, j
brother of the bride, gave her into the j
keeping of the groom, and Charles Vo- i
gelbang officiated in the capacity of best
man.
The bride wore an elegant black tailor
made gown, lined with white satin. Her
hat was a stylish black chenille, walk
ing shape, and trimmed with a bunch of
black plumes.
Mr. and Mrs. Vogelsang left yesterday
afternoon for Coronado, where they will
spend their honeymoon. On their return
they will reside at the California Hotel.
The second meeting this season of the
fashionable Cinderella dancing class was
held last evening at Native Sons* Hall.

THE SAX FRANCISCO CALL. THFESDAY, JANUARY 12, 1809.
WILL BE THE PASTOR
OF BROOKLYN CHURCH
Rev. Hugh W. Fraser, Formerly a Missionary
to China, Receives a Unanimous Call.
THE members of the Brooklyn Prebyterian Church, East Oakland, met
last evening and extended a unanimous call to the Rev. Hugh \V. Fraser of
the Olivet Presbyterian Church, Minneapolis. Minn., to become their pastor.
This is the pulpit vacated six months of more ago by the Rev. S. 8. Palmer,
when hn accepted a call to Columbus, Ohio.
Mr. Fraser was a minister in Oakland during 1 the month of December, and
occupied the pulpit during his stay. His delivery is of a conversational style, en
tirely extemporaneous but very forcible. He Is said to be an equal of Dr. Coyle
of the First Church in delivery. The call was made on recommendation of Rev.
C. C. Herriott. who has known Mr. Fraser and his work for years.
Mr. Fraser was born in Nova Scotia, X. 8., thirty-fight years ago. He was
graduated from the Manitoba College and Theological School, at Winnipeg, and
began his clerical labors at Fort Williams, Manitoba, where he remained two
years. He then went to China as a missionary under the American Board, but
his health failed after three y?.r< w>rk and he was compelled to return to his
native country. After a short pastorate at Morehead, Minn.. Mr. Fraser was called
to Minneapolis. There he had a church of 400 members, with a Sunday-school
of 500 children. He has been there four years, and is spoken of in the highest
terms.
The Brooklyn church officials feel confident that Mr. Fraser will accept the
call and remove to this State at an early date.
TO PUT NEW LIFE IN
AN OLD LOCALITY
A MOVE TO REVIVE BUSINESS
NEAR THE WATER FRONT.
Modern Buildings, Streets and Sew
ers for What Was Once the
Wholesale Section.
A movement is on foot, backed by some
of the wealthiest property-owners in the
city, to revive the old wholesale district
lylr.g east of Kearny and north of Cali
fornia streets. An important meeting to
this end was held yesterday in the Mills
building and an organization which will
likely to be called the "Central North End
Improvement Association" was created.
Henry P. Sonntag presided and Edgar
Painter acted as secretary. Mr. Sonntag
stated the objects of the gathering and
what is desired to be accomplished. He
said that several years ago the district
mentioned was the wholesale center of
the city, but that it has been allowed to
get behind the times. The assessed valu
ation is about J00.0U0.000 and the property
is owned by about 960 people, the majority
being the wealthiest in the State. For all
that the district has been allowed to* go
to decay in the matter of streets, sidewalks,
houses, etc., and the district is assessed
as high as in its most prosperous days.
Rents of all kinds have gone down to
almost nothing.
The chairman said that in view of the
recent addition of Hawaii and the other
Pacific territotries the water front of this
city is soon to become very valuable and
property adjoining will increase in value
( orrespondingly. If the property-owners
will act in concert in the matter of get
ting modern basalt and bitumen pave
ments, good sewerage and sidewalks,
(>an up the old buildings and put in
modern improvements and get the rail
road to extend an electric line into the
district there is sure to be a good demand
for what is now not paying taxes. Mr.
Bonntag said that he is Informed that the
increased shipping, resulting from l.'ncle
Sam's recent acquisitions, will bring many
new business houses into this city and it
stands to reason that this naturally fav
ored district will again be the wholesale
locality if the owners will put their
premises and streets in order.
After deciding to set the old historic
district on its feet again the meeting
ted the chairman to appoint a com
mittee of five to frame a constitution and
by-lawa for the new organization. The
body will consist of a president, three
vice presidents, a secretary and an ex- â ;
tlve committee of eleven. The latter will
: divided into committees on public
improvement, private Improvement, judi
ciary and legislation. Quite a number
signed the roll and the secretary was di
d to see about 400 property-owners
who have assured the promoters that they
will join the association. The dues of the
small owner? will be about $1 per month
and th>^ larger in proportion. The pro
priety of asking the Legislature to grant
a franchise for a belt railway line around
the water front was discussed ar.d left
over until the next meeting.

DARING BURGLARY
ON HAVES STREET
BUTCHER STORE OF GEORGE E.
GOLDEN LOOTED.
Thieves Lighted Candles and Worked
With No Apparent Fear of
the Police.
A daring robbery occurred during the
early hours of yesterday morning in the
butcher store of George E. Golden, 572
Hayes street. Although the articles tak
en are not of great financial value their
loss is keenly felt by the proprietor of
the store, and the fact is also evident that
the thieves must have worked at least
thirty minutes in the store where they
could plainly be seen from the street.
The thieves entered the store by cut
ting away the wire netting which has
been substituted for glass In one of the
front windows in order to Insure good
ventilation. They then took almost every
thing movable out of the store, even to
the scales used for weighing the meat. A
quantity of Swiss cheese, hams, smoked
beef, as well as some fresh meat and a
new rubber coat belonging to Mr. Gold
en were carried away. There is a safe
in the back part of the store, which is
never used, and the door stands open.
The thieves pulled all the drawers out of
this and threw them on the floor. That
they worked openly is shown by the fact
that candle grease was pkmifullly scat
tered over everything and themudd'y foot
prints indicate that there were several
men in the store.
It is supposed that the burp'.arv oc
curred at about 3 o'clock in the morning, 'â 
as the people living upstairs say chat they
heard some one moving around in the
store at that hour. As the noise cont,
for a long time they did not suspect any- '<
thing out of the way. Where the police
were at this hour is a mystery. It was j
raining heavily at the time, anci It is just
possible that the guardians of the peace
bad found shelter from the downpour.
Mr. Golden thinks the burglary was
committed by one of the gangs of hood
lums which infeat the back alleys in that ;
part of the city. He does not believe they
had a wagon, but is of the opinion that
the plunder was carried away in sacks.
The matter was reported to the police
and a promise was made that it would
be fully investigated. Mr. Golden would j
appreciate an explanation from the of
fior who was supposed to be guarding :
property at that hour.

NOTEJ3 OF THE WEATHER. [
Storm Has Caused Heavy Bain in All
Parts of the State.
Professor Hammon said yesterday that '
the storm which has been raging for the !
past two days has caused heavy rains !
throughout the entire State. -with ;
snow in the mountains above 2500 '
feet elevation. The storm, which was at
fint central off the coast of Oregon, has
been apparently divided. A part of it
moved southeast and la now central in
Western Arizona. The remainder has
moved northeast and is now central In j
British Columbia.
The heaviest rainfall reported Is at Red â¢
Bluff, where about five inches fell dur
ing the storm. In Southern California
nearly two inches has fallen. It has
been impossible to get the customary re
ports on account of the high winds which
have blown down the telegraph lines
From the data that could be obtained,
however. Professor Hammon says thai'
judging from the barometric conditions'
which prevail, the storm will remain with
us and there will be shower? to-day with
brisk to high southeast changing to >
southwest winds.
The rise in the Sacramento River is 'â 
still going on at Colusa, and there was
seventeen inches of water In the chan
nel yesterday. Unless heavy rains should
continue there is no danger of an over
flow.
The minimum temperatures reported
were: San Diego. 52; Los Angeles and
Yuma, 46: San Luis Obispo and Sacra
mento, 42; Red Bluff. 40: San Francisco
and Fresno, 44; Mount Tamalpais. 3&

THE EIGHTH
WILL LEAVE
THE SERVICE
Men Want to Retire to
Private Life.
BUT THE OFFICERS DO NOT
COL. HENSHAW TRIED COL. BER
RY'S PLAN TO NO PURPOSE.
Major General Shafter Will Arrive
Here Next Tuesday and Relieve
Major General Merriam
of the Command.
The fate of the Eighth California Regi
ment has been decided. It Is to leave the
service without having had the honor of
smelling powder or facing the enemy. A3
recently predicted in The Call, the regi
ment is to be mustered out without delay.
When the order first came from the War :
Department to Major General Merriam !
ordering that the regiment be mustered ;
out, Colonel Henshaw protested on the !
ground that a majority of the officers and
men preferred to remain in the service.
Following the example of Colonel John R.
Berry of the Seventh California Kegi
ment he invoked the aid of Senators
White and Perkins, with the result that
General Merriam was ordered to take a
poll of the regiment for the purpose of ;
ascertaining the wishes of the men of the
regiment. The poll showed that while a :
majority of the officers desired to remain
in the service, the men were almost unan- j
imous in the wish to be mustered out.
There will be no delay in mustering the :
regiment out. Captains Pratt and Danes
of the Third Artillery and Captain Tay
lor of the Fourteenth Infantry will per- !
form the work. The different companies !
will be mustered out at the various sta
tions at which they are now serving.
Major General Merriam and his aid.
First Lieutenant John B. Bennett, will j
leave next week for Denver. It was ex- j
pected that Major General Shafter would |
have been here on Tuesday to assume i
command of the department. General
Merriam received a dispatch to that ef
fect from him last week, but for s<..me
reason his departure from New York has
Let-n delayed until to-day. General Mer
riam said yesterday that General Shaf
ter would arrive here next Tuesday. He '
will as soon as he arrives assume com
mand of the department.
The Twentieth Infantry will not arrive
here until about the 25th inst. The trans
port Scandia will not sail until the 27th,
as she cannot be got ready before that
time. It is not thought that any more
regulars besides the Twentieth Infantry
will be sent to Manila from this point for
the present. The Morgan City, which will
sail with the Scandia, may be used to
bring back returning volunteer troops, '<
possibly a portion of the First California
Regiment.
THEY TOUGHT TOR COIN.
Two Alleged Amateurs Attempted a
Hippodrome at the Merchants'
Club.
If the Merchants' Athletic Club, with
headquarters on the corner of Davis and
Washington streets, intend to continue
giving members' monthly boxing enter
tainments it must keep a close watch on
the hippodromers.
Last evening Billy Otts, the young man
of curly locks and effeminate features, at
tempted to pull off a little prearranged af
fair with the assistance of an ex-messen
ger boy known as Jack Kenny. The last
named would-be boxer was billed to meet
Otts in an eight-round contest, but he
considered that one round would satisfy
the spectators and he gently dropped to
the floor just before the gong announced
the end of the first engagement. Harry
McGinnis. the referee, quickly detected
the fraud and he announced it a "no con.
test" and declared all bets off. The con
testantsâsupposed to be amateursâ it is
alleged were rignting for $250 a side, and
on this account some interest was taken
in the contest. A pickaninny pool selier.
who is seldom heard of in the sporting
world sold pools on the fake with Otts
a favorite at 10 to 3. The newsboys un
derstood how the saheme was arranged
and they- plunged on Otts. when much to
their disgust the referee declared all
bets off.
After a few friendly bouts between
members, Dick O'Mera and "Doc" Fiynn
boxed six hard rounds and Flynn was de
clared the victor. This was the only re
deeming feature of the show.

COMMERCIAL WORLD.
SUMMARY OF THE MARKETS.
Silver unchanged.
Wheat steady. Barley quiet.
Oats, Corn and Rye dull.
Fran and Middlings advanced sharply.
Nothing doing in Hay.

Putter tending downward. Eggs very firm.
Fruit market dull.
Meats and Provisions unchanged.

NEW YORK MONEY MARKET.
The circular of Henry Clews says, under date
of New York, January 7: We have had the
first week's taste of a financial year that Is
anticipated with more than ordinary hopeful
ness. It has been in some measure interrupted
by the holidays, and comes too early to receive
the full impulse of the extraordinary disburse
ments of January Interest and dividends on
Fecurttles. According to careful estimates,
these payments at New York alone amount this
month to about $110,000,000 which is far beyond
all precedent. This fact affords the best pos
sible confirmation of the general impression
that the past year has been one of general and
unusual prosperity. These payments must in
evitably have an effect upon the market for
Fecurities; for. apart from their encouraging
ppeculatlve operations, they must be attended
â¢with considerable reinvestment of the earnings;
Â«t the moment, however, it Is too early to look
for the full effect of these Influences upon the
market. â¢
We have now a fair prospect of a period of
decided ease in money extending over some
months. Apart from the drift of currency from
the.. Interior usual at this season, symptoms in
dicate .i probability of the renewals of imports
of Bold. There Is less disposition toward the
temporary employment of funds In foreign bills
of exchange; and, so soon as those Investments
materially abate, an influx of gold would be
almost inevitable. Within the past week, $1,
--6(AOf'O of the metal have been engaged for
shipment from London to this city; which has
teen due, however, rather to a fall In gold
o broad than to lower rates for bills on this
market.

EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK. Jan. 11.â Directors' meetings :
were responsible for considerable variations in \
the character of the stock speculation to-day.
As la usually the case forecasts were rife as
to the probable action of the various director- !
ies In session which were utilized for effect j
upon the market value of securities interested. !
The most Important development was the ac- i
tion of the Louisville and Nashville directors
In replacing the stock in the category of divi
dend payers. The report submitted for the
six months ending December 31 showed the
total Income as $4, 437. â¬22 and a surplus of I
$1,426,739, after deducting fixed charges and i
sinking fund requirements. This report I
prompted the management to declare a dlvi- |
dend of IVj per cent out of the earnings for
that period, which may foreshadow a regular
annual disbursement of 3 per cent. The divi- :
dends on this property were suspended In 1593, j
and a reduction of 10 per cent was made In
the salaries of employes, with a proviso that |
they would be restored when earnings war- j
ranted it. This promise was carried out last
year, and the successive favorable character '
of the traffic statements published ,arouse ex
pectations with the Investment public that the
dividends would be soon declared on the stock. I
The proceedings of the Sugar directors were '
also viewed with Interest, as it was inti- ,
mated that some publicity would be given of :
the result of the company's operations In the .
pending trade war. The stockholders' ratifi
cation of the action of the directors in voting !
"to reserve the profits of the company as work-
Ing capital was interpreted by the bears as
indicating a possible abandonment of dividend
payments. The stock was thrown on the mar
. ket In large volumes, and sufficient to check
the aggressive upward movement which was
in progress on the announcement that the Sen- '
ate committee had agreed to report without !
amendment the peace treaty. The market In
general presented other features of striking in
terest. Including as it did an outburst of In- {
vestment buying which improved the standard I
r hares from 1 to 4 points. The Vanderbilt |
Stocks were unusually prominent and a burst
of animation and strength which lifted Perm- '
sylvania 4V.. exercised a healthful influence on
the entire market. The specialties, aside from
Sugar, moved in comparatively narrow limits
and figured very moderately In the day's total.
Early weakness in which Plttsburg, Cincin
nati. Chicago and St. Louis made a sensational
break of 6>^ gave the bears a temporary ad
vantage, but the bulls got the whip-hand in |
the subsequent dealings and closed the mar- '
ket well up to the best at substantial gains.
London' operations were considerably reduced
on both sides of the account. The market for
bonds developed a sagging tendency, where
recessions were not significant except In iso
lated cases. United States 6s coupon declined
**, in the bid price.

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
! CHICAGO, Jan. 11.â Reports of liberal offer
ings of wheat from Argentine and Russia, to
gether with easier cables from Liverpool, soon
gave the market here a weaker tone at the out
' set. Domestic receipts were light compared
'. with their volume a month ago, but when set
, against receipts at the corresponding time last
year they were, from the point of view of the
i bulls, still very discouraglngly heavy. Under
i these Influences May opened %@%c lower, at
! from 7u^i37oVtC, and in less than half an hour
it could be bought at 69% c. That was under
, "put" price, and there arose a good demand
| from holders of these privileges, who were dis
| posed to take profits. Corn about this time be
i gan to show recuperative strength after earlier
weakness, and the buying of wheat became suf
flently urgent to cause a quick recovery of
May to TO^c. When the clearances from the
Atlantic poru for the day were placed at 1,204,
--000 bushels, it caused a quick advance to 70% c.
New York reported a good Inquiry for wheat
from the Continent, and claimed fifty boatloads
have been disposed of. May sold as high rip
"lS'l^c, and closed at 71c buyers. 1/
Large receipts, lower cables and the dcclii*
in wheat weakened corn early. It rallied laÂ»r
on predictions of unsettled weather and clear
â  ances of nearly a million bushels. The market
i continued strong after the first dip, and closed
j at top price. May gained %c.
A good demand from the country and small
i offerings strengthened oats. The sharp ad
vance in corn near the end was reflected in
oats, and the market closed strong, with a
gain of Vl@%c.

mand strengthened provisions. Orders from
the outside were numerous on the buying slda
and . commission houses were also taking on

BOSTON WOOL MARKET.
BOSTON, Jan. 11.â The American Wool and
i Cotton Reporter will say to-morrow: The wool
I market Is quiet and almost without feature
â of Interest. The failure 1 of the Aesabet Woolen
I Mills and the opening of heavy-weight goods
have occupied most of the attention of the
trade. The prices at which goods are offered,
which range from 6 to 10 per cent below laÂ«t
season's figures, are regarded by the wool
trade as establishing a solid foundation for a
future appreciation In values for manufactured
products, and thus affording a pretty good
< assurance that there Is to be this season an
i absence of cancellations from the clothing
i trade.
The season Is opening slowly, but that In
! itself is rather a favorable feature than other
wise.
Sales of the week In Boston amount to
1 2 647. 000 pounds domestic and 663,. r ioo pounds
foreign, making a total of 3.210.R00. against a
total of 3. 796. 0fH) for the previous weeek and a
total of 1,308,000 for the corresponding wwk
last year Bales since January 1. 1599. amount
to 7,006,500 pounds, against 10,958,000 last year
at this time.

THE STOCK MARKET.
There were few changes In mining stocks
either way.
Local securities were quieter, with less fluc
tuation.
The Julia Con assessment falls delinquent In
board to-day.
The stock dividend declared by the Yellow
Jacket Mining Company at the rate of 35
shares for each 100 shares outstanding, will be
payable to stockholders on and after January
12.
The Alaska Packers' Association held its an
nual meeting on January 10 and elected the fol
lowing board of directors: Henry F. Fort
mann, president; Charles Hirsch and Sidney M.
Smith, vice presidents; W. B. Bradford, secre
tary; D. Drysdale, assistant secretary; E. B.
Pond, G. Niebaum, Isaac Liebes and George
W. Hume, directors; Wells, Fargo & Co. 'a
Bank, treasurer. The company has paid
monthly dividends uninterruptedly for over
five years. Those for the calendar year of IS9B
amounted to $432,000. The first dividend in
1899 will be paid to-day and amounts to $36,000,
or at the rate of 75c per shaxe. The stock sold
yesterday at 107, or 7 per cent premium.
At the annual meeting of the Crocker-Wool
worth National Bank on the 10th, the old board
of directors was re-elected, including W. H.
Crocker. W. E. Brown, G. W. Kline, C. E.
Green. G. W. Scott, H. J. Crocker and E. B.
Pond. W. H. Crocker was re-elected president,
W E. Brown vice president, G. W. Kline cash
ier and the office of assistant cashier was
created and filled by the election of Welling
ton Gregg Jr.
The Fireman's Fund Insurance Company has
I declared the usual 3 per cent quarterly divi
! dend, payable this month.
At the annual meeting of the Alice Mining
I Company of Utah the Old directors were re
| elected, with Joseph R. Walker as president
! and Louis H. Farnsworth secretary. This
j company has paid $1,075,000 In dividends to
{ date.
The annual meeting of the stockholders of
the First National Bank of San Francisco was
held on January 10. The old board of direc
tors was re-elected, composed of S. G. Mur
phy, James Moffltt, George A. Low, George C.
Perkins, James D. Phelan. Thomas Jennings,
J. W. Van Bergen, J. Downey Harvey and
John A. Hooper. S. G. Murphy was re-elected
president, James Moffitt vice president, James
K. Lynch cashier and J. K. Moffitt assistant
cashier. The post year was a prosperous one.
The gross prorlts amounted to $347,808 03. The
net profits were $243,591 52, and two dividends
amounting to $150,000, or at the rate of 10 per
cent per annum were paid. The surplus amounts
to $1, 111,796 84.
At the annual meeting of tho Pacific Coast
Savings Society on the 10th, the old board of
directors was re-elected as follows: Wendell
Eaatoo, C. S. Benedict. George Easton, H.
Francis and William C. Murdoch. Wendell
Kaston was re-elected president. C. S. Bene
dict vice president and W. P. Henry secretary.

pany reports a cash indebtedness of $107 42 on
January 1, with a balance of $8202 90 due on
i the pending assessment. The outstanding lia
bilities of the company are placed at $10,568.

NOTICE TO iIAiiINERS.
A branch of the United States Hydrographlc
Office, located in the Merchants" Exchange, Is
maintained In San Francisco for the benefit of
mariners, without regard to nationality and j
free of expense.
Navigators are cordially Invited to visit the
Office, where complete sets of charts and sail
ing directions of the world are kept on hand i
for comparison and reference, and the latest '
Information can always be obtained regarding
lights, dangers to navigation and all matters
01 Interest to ocean commerce.
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
building, at the foot of Market street. Is hoisted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
noon. 12'ith meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived each day from the United States Naval
Observatory. Mare Island. Cal.
A notice stating whether the ball was dropped
on time or giving the error. If any. Is published
In the morning papers the following day.
W. S HUGHES,
Lieutenant. TT. S. N.. In charge.

NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given In the left
hand column and the successive tide? of the
day in the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide of
the day, the third time column the third tlds
Rnd the last or right band column gives the
last tide of the day. except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
given are additions to the soundings on the
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â ) precedes the height,
and then the number given Is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference is tVe mann o^ the Inwi" 1 low writer*.

TREATY OF PEACE IS
REPORTED FAVORABLY
WASHINGTON, Jan. 11.â The Senate Committee on Foreign Re
lations to-day authorized Senator Davis to report the peace treaty
favorably without amendment. The committee was in session for only
an hour, and the greater part of this time was consumed in waiting
for a quorum, the Senators meantime discussing informally the pro
visions of the treaty.
There was only a bare quorum of the committee present, but
Senators Cullom and Clarke, who were among the absentees, sent
their proxies. Senators Morgan and Gray were the only Democrats
in attendance, but the other Democratic membersâ Senators Daniel,
Mills and Turpieâ had full notice of the purpose of the meeting, and
they indicated no opposition to immediate action.
There was no suggestion of any amendment in committee. Sen
ator Davis was instructed to press the treaty upon the attention of
the Senate, with a view to securing as early action as possible. The
committee also authorized Senator Davis to report favorably upon
the proposition to remove the injunction of secrecy.
During the executive session of the Senate Berry moved that the
debate upon the treaty should be conducted In open Senate, but the
motion went over until to-morrow. There were only casual remarks
upon the policy of publicity, and these were generally favorable to
the removal of the secrecy injunction from the treaty and as gener
ally opposed to the c-pening of the doors of the Senate for the debate
upon the treaty.
Senator Davis gave notice that he would ask for the earliest pos
sible consideration, giving the impression that he would move to
take up the treaty to-morrow.
of Nebraska, by some remarks he made
upon a resolution he had introduced.
Hoar of Massachusetts and Gray of

Bacon of Georgia offered the follow
ing Joint resolution:
1 Resolved, That the Government and the
people of the United States have not waged
the recent war with Spain tor conquest and for

S. That whereas, at the time of thÂ» declara

pine Islands were actively engaged in a war

ers from the consent of the governed, the
Government of the United -States recognizes
and that the people of the Philippine Islands
of a right ought to be free and Independent;
that with this view and to give effect to the.
same the Government of the United States has
required the Government of Spain to relinquish
its authority and government In the Philippine
Islands and to withdraw its land and naval
forces from the said Philippines and from the

4. That the United States hereby disclaim
any disposition to exercise sovereignty. Juris
diction or control over the said Islands, and

ent government shall have been duly erected

Allen introduced a resolution declaring

pinos would be unwarranted, and in a

gress. .
Foraker was thrn recognized ana pro
ceeded to deliver his set speech on the
general question of the power to extend
our territory. An assertion of the tem
porary character of our possession "f the
Philllipines oreattd a stir in the chamber.
Forak- r reiterated it. We could nut <1p-
Bert tho people of the islands, he said,
and subject them to the risks of disorder,
anarchy, misrule and mob rule while they
might * 1"' still unfit for self-govern
ment. But our occupation was not to be
permanent. "I do not understand that
any one desires anything- but the ultimate
Indepi ' the people of the Philip
pines," paid he, emphatically, "neither tne
nor any on* in this chamber.
"But what about our rignt if we chose
to hold them permanently with no
thought of their ultimate independence?"
Inquired Hoar.
'â¢We have an unquestioned right to do
so," replied Foraker. "I speak particu
larly of our legal right."
"What of the statement about hauling
down the American flag?" interjected
Hoar.
"No one desires to retain the Philippines
permanently," replied Foraker again.
"The President is as much a lover of lib
erty, truth and justice as is the Senator
from Massachusetts, and hi? love of lib
erty goes out to tho people of the Philip
pines as unerringly as to his own."
"Then we are to understand the state
ment that the American flag is not to be
hauled down." said Hoar, "does not mean
that we are to hold perpetual domain. If
the people of the Philippines believe their
happiness can be best secured by self
government they are to be given an op
portunity to govern themselves?"
"With the determination of the ultimate
policy respecting the Philippines," replied
Foraker, "their feelings will have much
to do. No one, so far as 1 am able to
learn Is prepared by force and violence
to take and hold tiinm. I am willing to
trust the Administration. I have no sym
pathy with those who talk of making war
on Aguinaldo any more than I had with
those who talked of making war on Go
mez."
"Thank God for that," murmured Hoar
from his seat, but in a Voice audi
ble through the chamber.
"I repeat," <<intinue<3 Foraker, "that we
had four choices as to what we should
do. and I affirm that the President acted
wisely. Jt is now for Congress to in
vestigate whether the Filipinos are capa
ble of self-"government and whether they
desire self-government. We will know
in a short time what the feeling of the
population of the islands is. Then we
can act intelligently, and I know we will
act justly."
In conclusion, Foraker said he would
place In the record the recent proclama
tion of the President to the insurgents as
a declaration of the spirit and purpose of
the United States. When he took his seat
there was a demonstration of approval
from the galleries, which was unchecked
by the presiding officer.
At the conclusion of Foraker's speech

BATTLESHIP IOWA IS
COMING TO THIS PORT
WASHINGTON, Jan. 11.â The War
Department is negotiating for a charter
of the steamer Centennial, now at San
Francisco, to carry stores and com
missary supplies to Manila.
Captain Barker to-day cabled the
Navy Department from Callao, Peru,
that he was about to sail with the Ore
gon, the Scandia and the Iris directly
for Honolulu on the way to Manila.
At the same time the battle-ship lowa,
which had accompanied the Oregon
around from New York, started north
ward for San Francisco in company
with the collier Justin and the supply
steamer Celtic, her voyage being about
4000 miles in length.
THE SAX FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, JANUARY 12, 1899.
WHEN TROOPS MAY
BE MUSTERED OUT
Call Office, Rigga House,
Washington, Jan. 11.
The Call correspondent In
quired at the War Depart
ment to-day whether the situ
ation in the Philippines was
likely to postpone the muster
ing out of the First California
and other volunteer regiments.
Adjutant General Corbin said:
"It has been our purpose to
muster out the volunteer regi
ments as rapidly as possible, for
they were enlisted to serve dur
ing the war with Spain. Strictly
speaking the war cannot be said
to have ended until the treaty of
peace is ratified, but to all in
tents and purposes it ceased
weeks ago. We appreciated the
fact that the soldiers in the
Philippines, so far away from
home, had endured many priva
tions and were anxious to give
them preferment It was and is
yet our intention to muster out
the Philippine volunteers as
soon as practicable. But you
can see as well as I that nothing
definite can be promised. Regu
lars will leave New York in a
few days for Manila, and if by
that time affairs there are tran
quil or comparatively bo several
volunteer regiments will be mus
tered out. They will be relieved
In the order In which they were
assigned to service there. In
this case the First California will
be one of the first to be dis
charged. This Is all I can say."
FIRM STAND AS
TO PHILIPPINES
No Nonsense to Be
Taken From Natives.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK, Jan. 11.â The Herald's
Washington correspondent sends the
following: Major General Otis cabled
to the War Department to-day that no
ohange had occurred in the situa
tion in the Philippines. It is under
stood the authorities have been
told the wishes of the Filipinos respect
ing the future government of the Isl
ands by the representatives of Aguin
aldo, who conferred yesterday with the
American officers, and careful con
sideration was given them during the
conference held at the White House
this afternoon by the President and
Secretary of War.
What the result of the confernce was
could not be learned, but there is
reason to believe the administration
will not budge from its position to es
tablish peace and order in the islands.
The Filipinos are to be impressed with
the good-will and friendship which the
United States has for them.
But there must be recog
nition o? and obedience to
the sovereignty of the
United States.
General Otis' action In appointing
commissioners to meet representatives
of the insurgents Is approved in official
circles here. It is declared that it does
not necessarily follow that the In
surgents are thereby recognized as
belligerents and that the United States
is treating them as such. The confer
ence was held to impress the natives
with the friendly purpose of the United
States. This policy will be continued
until a satisfactory agreement is
reached, which will mean obedience to
the desires of the United States or
until the treaty of peace is ratified,
when a different policy may be put in
force.
The friends of Germany are much in
censed by the report that the Berlin
Government is secretly aiding Aguin
aldo. It Is true the attitude of Ger
many several months ago gave rise to
apprehension to the Administration,
but has since given strong indications
of her intentions to be strictly neutral,
and has gone so far as to decline to
permit her consular representatives In
the Philippines to take care of the in
terests of Spanish subjects.
Germany, to quote a gen
tleman with whom I talked,
is too friendly disposed
toward the United States
to take any untoward
steps regarding the Philip
pines.
It may be that Aguinaldo is obtaining
foreign support, and the authorities
are looking into the subject. As soon
as the treaty of peace Is ratified, the
administration will be in a position to
protest to Great Britain or China, in
case any further filibustering expedi
tions start from their Jurisdiction for
the archipelago.
FRANCE SUBMITS AN
ULTIMATUM TO CHINA
Demands Cash Indemnity and
Certain Mining Privileges for
the Murder of a Priest.
VANCOUVER, B. C, Jan. 11.â According to mail advices by the
steamship Empress of Japan, arriving here to-day, France has made
demands on China In connection with the death of the priest. Father
Fleury, which if not acceded to mean, if not actual war, very
strained relations between the two countries which may ultimately
result in war. Father Fleury was some time ago captured by ban
dits and is now reported dead. The Chinese Government was warned
by the brigand chief that if soldiers were sent after him he would
behead the French priest. Notwithstanding this threat Chinese troops
advanced toward tMe brigands' stronghold. The priest was murdered,
but the rebel chief, Yuman-Tse, was afterward gTanted high honors
as a reward for laying down his arms.
The French Government officials in China has now made a for
mal demand for 5,000,000 taels indemnity for the death of the priest,
and also demands from China mining privileges in the rich placer
district of Yunnan, from which the French recently were driven
out.
Should China refuse to accede to France's demands forthwith,
the French Minister at Shanghai informs the Chinese Government,
a military expedition will without delay be sent into the interior of
China, via Yunnan and Szechuan. When the Empress of Japan left
Hongkong the ultimatum of the French Minister was still under con
sideration. Editorial comments in the vernacular press are to the
effect that China will never yield to the demands of France, par
ticularly as regards indemnity for the death of the priest. The at
tempt of France to seize territory in the Yang-Tse "Valley recently
aggravated the already bad feeling between the two countries and the
late insolent demands have, the papers say, brought matters almost
to a climax.
China may accede to the demand for mining privileges, but will
not take any responsibility for the death of Father Fleury. One pa
p^r says: "The situation is very strained and we now look for the
worst."
RUSSIANS AND
JAPS IN BATTLE
Conflict Between Ma
rines at Nagasaki.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
VANCOUVER, B. C, Jan. 11.â Mail
advices by the steamship Empress of
Japan, which arrived here from the
Orient to-day, tell of a desperate fight
Which occurred in the middle of De
cember between Russian marines, sixty
in number, and a large squad of Japan
ese marines belonging to the torpedo
department.
The Russians had landed at Fuchi
Inasa, Nagasaki, and after military ex
ercises adjourned in a body to a mili
tary inn, where they drank freely. After
several hours spent ashore in this man
ner the officer in command gave an
order to return to the 3hip, saying at
the same time if any â¢ Japanese got
in the way to cut them down.
Unfortunately a squad of very or
derly Japanese marines were just en
tering the city by the main ro-ad, which
the Russians were taking to leave. The
thoroughfare being narrow they cau
tiously formed in single file and were
about to pass qnietly by the Russians,
when the captain of the latter, with a
curse, ordered them to turn back.
The little brownies refused the re
quest of the Czar's bluejackets, and a
small lieutenant boldly strode up to
the Russian captain and slapped him in
the face, at the game time calling upon
him for an apology. A desperate hand
to-hand fight ensupd, in which about
twenty men on both sides w r ere badly
cut and bruised. The Japs, although
outnumbered two to one, held the Rus
sians in check until reinforcements ar
rived from the city in the shape of stu
dents from the gymnasium. With poles
and clubs the Russians were belabored
until they screamed for mercy. They
fled back to the city and demanded pro
tection at the Japanese barracks, which
was grudgingly granted them. Public
feeling is very strong against the Rus
sians and an investigation is demanded.
It is stated that two Japs will die from
injuries.

ALASKA APPROVED
WASHINGTON. Jan. 11.â The HÂ«use to
day completed and passed the bill for the
codification of the criminal laws of Alas
ka, upon winch it has been working in
termittently for a week.
An amendment was adopted providing
a high license system* in the. Territory,
with a epecles of local option. Liquor

cense of $1-0 a year, and the consent of
> the majority of the white citizens residing

VENTURA COUNTY
GETS A WETTING
First Genuine Rain in
Three Years.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
HUENEME, Jan. 11.â The first genuine
rainstorm that has Moss^l this valley
since 1595 began here at daybreak yester
day and continued almost without cessa
tion till sundown, during which time 4.15
inches fell. At Oxnard, five miles inland,
4.32 inches of precipitation was registered.
The mountains surrounding the valley
are covered with snow to their very base
linos. This afternoon the storm broke
out afresh, with the wind blowing briskly I
from the southeast, and the barometer
falling steadily. Indications are that the
rainfall for the storm will reach six
inches, making it a record-breaker for
this section of the State.
It 1? t^timated by conservative business
men here that the benefit to be derived
from the storm can easily be valued at
(2,000,000 fur this county. It is stated that
the sugar factory at Oxnard will at once
be enlarged to douMi: its present capacity,
thus affording facilities for the crushing
of -'"<> tons of beets dally.
Barley hay was selling here at $2o a
ton previous to the storm, but since the
fears of another dry year entertained by
farmers have been so gloriously dissipated
the price will be reduced considerably!
The storm will have the eIT lucing
the price of lima beans to a figure that ;
will awaken the market from its long :
lethargy and invite Eastern trade. The
acreage that will be planted to wheat and |
barley in Ventura County this season is
estimated at 55. UW acres; beans, 35,000
; sugar beets, 18,000 acres, and corn,
SUOO acres.
The plight of cattle men up to yester
day has Been a most deplorable one, ow
ing to the drought and lack of feed on the
ranges in the eastern extremity of the ;
county. In a single band of cattle over
SuO head died during the past two months.
the only sustenance to be found being that
afforded l.y the cactus plant. Men have '
been hired for the especial purpose of
cutting the plant and burning the thorns
in order to make the Spiny shrub fit for
fodder. In other parts the wholesale
slaughter of cattle has been going on to
end their suffering.
WOODLAND, Jan. 11.â At noon to-day
the rainfall for the storm amounted to
L 52 inches, making a total of 6.57 inches '
for the season. In the western part of the
county the rainfall has been much heav
ier. A dispatch from Rumsey says: "At
noon to-day the gauge showed aprecipita- :
tion of 2.16 inches for the storm. There
were intermittent showers during the af
ternoon. Tuesday night the horizon
cleared and the stars shone out clear and
bright. At this writing the skies are
overcast, the wind is in the right direc
tion for a continuation of the storm and i
a light sprinkle has already commenced.
The prospects are good for the heaviest i
storm for many years. The beneficial re- I
gults to the farmers and fruit-growers
of Capay Valley can hardly be overesti
mated. The hay crop Is already assured.
But little more rain Is needed to i:
good grain crops. A few weeks ago the
fruit trees were suffering in consequence
of the drought and there was grave dan
ger of a serious loss in trees as well as
short crops. Now all danger from that
source seems past and good crops are
reasonably sure. The people of Western
Yolo are encouraged to take a cheerful
view of the outlook."
SAN DIEGO, Jan. 11.â The storm which
commenced at noon here yesterday was
the hardest and most general downpour
throughout the county which has oc
curred within the past two years. The
storm was a steady downpour up to 7
p. m., when for half an hour it was a
veritable cloudburst. The rainfall in the
city for the storm was 1.31 inches, mak
ing 3.11 inches for the season, against OS
inches for the total season last year. The
heaviest rainfall of the storm in this vi
cinity was at Cuyamaca dam, where 3.60
Inches fell in eighteen hours. Small wash
outs occurred on the National City and
Otay and Santa Fe roads, but the trains
are running to-day.
MILTON, Jan. 11.â The heaviest storm
In several years prevailed here to-d:iy.
Last night rain fell to the depth of 1.33
inches. To-day rain fell heavily at in
tervals and a terrific wind amounting to
almost a gale blew from the southeast.
Roofs were denuded of their coverings
and windmills and fences blown down.
With this storm the last fear of a dry sea
son has vanished. Stages leaving points
In the mountains early in the morning to
connect with the afternoon train here
report an exceedingly heavy storm ln
the mountains and a largÂ« addition to the
supply of snow.
KING CITY, Jan. 11.â Rain has been
falling here for the past twenty-four
hours and everything indicates that the
season of 1599 will be a prosperous one
for Monterey County. The present storm
means thousands of dollars' increase in
<^rops and with even a reasonable amount
of rain between now and May will insure
plenty of water for the entire season and
a sufficient supply of grass on the ranges j
for the cattle.
SANTA BARBARA. Jan. 11.-Rain be- 1
gan falling again this afternoon, pre
ceded by a heavy hailstorm and thunder.
Hail covered the ground to a depth of
an inch, giving the appearance of snow.
Old and young were out playing snow
ball.
The rain began to descend again about
11 o'clock this morning and the weather
has been showery all afternoon. There
has been very little wind to-day.

general all over the county.

ALAMEDA IS INDIGNANT
"DANBTJRNSVILLE" IS NOT ALL
THE COUNTY.
Three of the Delegation Are Said to
Have Already Dug Their
Political Graves.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, Jan. 11.
Much dissatisfaction is being expressed
that the representatives In the jSenate
and Assembly whose districts include the
race track should be voting for Dan Burns
for United States Senator. Kad it not
been for the fact that the Municipal
League restricts Itself solely to purely lo
cal polities, these members would have
been censured ;it last night's session. The
town of Emeryville, which is called, and
probably with a greater degree of truth
than the average citiz/n is aware of,
"Dansburnsville," is in the Senatorial dis
trict of Frank Leavitt and the Assembly
district of A, A. McKeen.
it is now being said that these two j
men are representing no portion of their â¢
district but the racing town and totally i
ignoring the desires of the great majority â 
of voters outside Emeryville.
In the great register somewhere about \
nine hundred names are credited to Em
eryville, and prior to election it was so j
evident that one-half of this enormous !
registration was not above suspicion that
the Army and Navy Republican League
started an Investigatlona. For some rea
son or other the matter was not pressed, !
and since that time Emeryville's vote' has
'been regarded a.< somewhat doubtiul. It
is conceded on all hands that not five â 
per cent of the voters of Alameda Coun
ty, outside of the two or three hundred
who owe their very existence at the race .
track to Burns and his followers, are de
sirous of sending the colonel to the United
States Senate, The other Assemblyman,
Kelly, who voted for Burns, has also dug
his political grave, for although he rep
resents a Bectlon of the city that i.* al
ways credited with being a "warm" dis
trict politically, there is no desire in it to
honor Mr. Burns,
Alameda is essentially a conservative |
county, and although there is among its
many politicians a positive repugnance to
what is termed the interference of the
church in politics, it can generally be re
garded as fairly expressive of popular
opinion when the ministers of the vari
ous denominations express their views.
This they have done In very clear lan- '
?uage regarding Colonel Burns, and in the ,
ace of all the opposition to uÂ»e man and i
hi? methods, th> respectable element in
surprised that three members of the I
Legislative delegation should represent i
the race track and all its corruption rath
er than the balance of their respective dis
tricts.
WAR RECORD OF
"COLONEL" BURNS
While the "Tocsin" Act Was
On He Never Left the
State.
CALL HEADQUARTERS. SACRA
MENTO, Jan. 11.â "When the tocsin of
â¢war was sounded throughout the land he
shouldered his musket and hastened to his
country's call. And when a million men
laid down their arms he returned to the
land of his adoption and resumed the
peaceful pursuits of ordinary life."
Assemblyman Cobb of San Francisco,
placing the name of the man from Mexico
: before the Assembly as a candidate for
. United States Senator, spoke thus beau
tifully of the brilliant war record of
.Colonel Daniel M. Burns.
Senator Wolfe of San Francisco, plac
ing the colonel in nomination before the
joint convention of the Senate and As
sembly to-day, referred touchingly to the
fact that the colonel had "followed in the
ranks with those who responded to the
. call to ?:ivo the Union."
Assemblyman C'obb's very pretty sen
tence is somewhat spoiled by the facts.
The records in the office of the adjutant
general of California do not altogether
near Mr. Cobb out in his well rounded
sentence about the "tocsin of war" and
the colonel's connection with the said
toosin.
The records of the adjutant general's
office show that Daniel M. Burns enlist
ed in Company B, Fourth California In
fantry, in the city of Sacramento, on
March 29, 1884. This was the time at
which "he hastened to Ins country's call."
Bul hero is whÂ«re Assemblyman Cobb
er"red when hf said: "And when a million
men laid down their arms he returned to
the land of his adoption and resumed the
peaceful pursuits of ordinary life." This
sounds very nice. The only trouble with
-the sentence is that the facts are not
true', it was never necessary for the
colonel, when he laid down his musket,
tO "return to the land of his adoption"
for the reason that from the time the
In of war was sounded" for him up
to the time the tocsin ceased sounding he
never left the State of California and he
never took but one trip away from San
Francisco Bay.
Tlit> colonel was mustered in at Sacra
mento and mustered out at Drum bar
racks. .San Francisco. It was not neces
sary for him when he <juit to make any
long trip either by land or sÂ»-a to get
back to "the land of his adoption" for
the reason that he never at any time left
it.
Company B of the Fourth California In
fantry, in which Colonel Burns held the
rank of Corporal, was at Drum barracks
San Francisco, from December, 1863 to
April, lxtiT.. On the 4th of February, ISOS.
Corporal JJurns was transferred from
Company B to Company (' of the same
â regiment, and that company was sta
tion. -<i at .Los Angeles, Camp Cady, from
February, 1866, until the time of the mus
ter-out.
The Fourth California Infantry was or
ganized September 21. ISO, at Placerville
and Auburn. The flrst commander was
â¢ 'olonel H. M. Judah. He was succeeded!
by >'(.lonel James K. Curtis. The regiment
was Brst stationed at Auburn and five
companies were sent to Oregon to relieve
the regulars there. In 1882 five compan
ies were stationed ai ('amp Union, near
Sacramento. In May. 1862, headqu
were removed to Camp Latham at Los
Angeles. In September of 1862 they were
transferred to Drum barracks â¢
Francisco, and there the headquart.
the regiment remained during the
of its time nf si rvice. Here is \
Corporal Burns put in the time of his
enlistrr.'-nt.
But where did Corporal Burns fall heir
to the title of < 'olonel. which lie i
with such becoming dignity and grace""
He did not get it when the "tocsin of
war" was Bounding. The Corporal ob
tained that title by sounding the ;
of politics. January lit, l^su. General John
F. Bheehan of the Fourth Brigade of the
California National Guard, made him
Major and paymaster on his staff. May
25. 1882. General Tozier reappointed him
Then Major Burns dropped out of the ser
vice for a time, for the tocsin had sound
ed for him. But this time it was a dif
ferent kind of a tocsin. It was the tocsin
of the Grand Jury of Sacramento coun
ty which sounded twelve cou&ts against
Major Burns for embezzling the funds of
the people of the State of California,
â which, as Secretary of State, he had mis
appropriated. ,
But January 8, 1891. Governor H. H.
Markham gave to Major Burns the title
of Colonel, for he made him Inspector of
Rifle Practice on his Ptaif. After a ser
vice of two years and a half the Colonel
wearied of military honors, and on July
24. 1893, he laid down his arms, "and re
sumed the peaceful pursuits of ordinary
life" at the Oakland race track, with
the bookmakers, the touts and the sure
thing gamblers who gather there as vul
tures do about a decaying corpse.
CONFIDENCE HAS GONE
FROM THE BURNS CAMP
CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO, Jan. 11.â The air of confidence
which has been one of the principal feat
ures characterizing the Burns fight and
fighters has gone. To-night the touts, the
members of the Federal Brigade and the
professional politicians who have been
lined up here for ten days last past labor
ing for the colonel wore a very blue air.
The colonel himself received in the par
lors of the Golden Eagle and If anybody
desired to converse with him on conflden
i tial topics he was taken to a nearby
I room. The colonel is still firm in his de-
I termination not to be interviewed. He re-
I fuses tn talk to representatives of the
! press. Major McLaughlin was also non
â  communicative to-night. He denies that
he stated that Burns was to receive sev
enteen additional votes to-day. But the
major did say last evening that within
the next day or so Burns would receive
an added strength of that number of
votes. To-night the major says that he
: thinks Burns will have some more votes
to-morrow, but declines to state just how
many or where they are to come from.
The other Burns lieutenants are also talk
ing about the gains that the colonel will
make on the third ballot, but they decline
I to give any details or reasons for their
i prophecy.
Frank P. Flint, United States Attorney
for the Southern District of California,
arrived to-night. He claims that he is for
1 Bulla. but there are those who state that
; Mr. Flint's rÂ«al choice for Senator is
Daniel M. Burns.
IOWA MAY TAX FOREIGN
INSURANCE COMPANIES
PUBUQUE. lowa, Jan. 11.â Judge |
Phlras, in the United States District
Court, to-day rendered an opinion sus
taining the constitutionality of the lowa j
law taxing foreign insurance companies, j
The plaintiffs sought an injunction re- I
straining the collection of a 3Vi per cent \
tax levied under the new State law. They j
attacked the law on the ground that it j
was unconstitutional, with special refer
ence to the section of the Federal constl- i
Union prohibiting the enactment of legis
lation that would abrogate treaty rights
of other countries. The plaintiffs, who arc
citizens of England, claimed the treaty
with the United States provided that no
special tax or restriction be levied against
English citizens who may engage in busi
ness here. The questions raised in the
income tax case also were brought up.
Judge Shiras decides in favor of the
State of Iowa: holds that the point made
by the plaintiffs regarding the treaty is
weak, and declares the State could go
farther and pass a law shutting them out
entirely.
LIBERTY IS
GRANTED TO
A MURDERESS
Mrs. Anna Mentel Is
Admitted to Bail.
Oakland Office San Francieoo Call,
908 Broadway, Jan. 11.
Mrs. Anna Mentel was granted her lib
erty to-day on condition of putting up
bonds to the amount of $20,000. Her fa
ther will probably appear before Judge
Hall to-morrow with the bondsmen and
the young murderess will walk out of tho
County Jail.
Attorney W. W. Foote appeared before
Judge Hall this morning and made an ar
gument, in which he stated that from the
evidence in the case he was convinced
that Mrs. Mentel could not be convicted
of murder in the first degree and ho
therefore formally made a motion that
she be admitted to ball in any reasonable
sum which the court saw fit to decide
upon. Attorney Foote recited some of the
evidence in the case, stating that the de
fendant had voluntarily given herself up
to justice immediately after the shooting
and also rfÂ«ferring to the fact that the
physician who examined her at the hos
pital had testified that there were bruises
upon ht-r arm and body which must have
been produced by a struggle.
Mrs. Mentel's attorney called theeourt's
attention to the agreement with ex-Dis
tri'-t Attorney Snook, who prosecuted the
ease at the preliminary examination, by
which the prosecution admitted that a
conviction of murder in the first degree
was not possible.
Mr. Foote took a side Blap at Justice
Morris of Alameda, who is not possessed
of a certificate to practice law, but grad
uated to the bench from a Constable's
office. "Without wishing to cast any re
flection on his Honor Justice Morris of
Alameda," said Mr. Foote, "I do not think
It is exercising very good judgment for a
country Justice to hold the defendant
without bail -when advised by the Dis
trict Attorney that bail was permissi
ble."
District Attorney Allen did not deny
that his predecessor had agreed that the
case waji one in which bail might be ac
cepted, but he said that later develop
ments in the case rendered it highly
probable that a jury would bring in a
verdict In the highest degree.
He referred to the fact that Mentel had
been found lying with one hand In hlB
pocket and resting upon a bundle, which
went to show that he had been carrying
the bundle In the other arm and had made
no struggle.
Judge Hall said he had looked into the
evidence and was satisfied that he could
properly grant bail, which would be fixed
at $20,000.
Mrs. Mentel. when arraigned on the
charge of murder, was very self-possessed
and in a very firm voice answered "Not
guilty."
At the request of Mr. Foote, who said
that his associate counsel, Senator Earl,
was busy in Sacramento, a continuance
of one week was granted.
Clara Barton tells about the work
of the Red Cross in Cuba in next
Sunday's Call.

NEARLY RAN
THE STRING
OUT ON THEM
Martin Piloted Five
Straight Winners.
BUT TWO WERE FAVORITES
SUBVIVOB. WON THE PRESIDIO
SELLING STAKE.
Don't Skip Me Beat a Fast Bunch, of
Sprinters â Sardonic Captured
thÂ« Handicap â Ferxier
Scored.
The superb saddle work of "Streets"
Martin was the one redeeming feature of
the sport at Ingleslde yesterday. Ban
ning with Ferrler In the first event, Mar
tin piloted five stnaight winners past the
judges. He rode Red Glenn In the final
number and finished last of the trio that
started. All mounts looked alike to
"Skeets." Some were favorites and some
were not.
It was a hard day on favorite player*.
The going was very juicy, and but two of
the pronounced choices managed to splash
in front.
The Presidio selling stake, worth $700 to
the first horse, ovÂ«r one mile and a fur
long, was the select piece of the after
noon. Seven started, and Pat Dunne's
Survivor proved a handy winner over Ob
sidian. The two colts named were equal
favorites in the betting. Martin, on Sur
vivor, soon had his mount in front, and at
the wire finished two lengths in advance
of the Burns & "Waterhouse entry-. Lava
tor was a fair third.
The talent sent Castake to the post fa
vorite for the opening six furlong run.
If he ran as fast as he could, then the
Apache gelding won't do. In a drive Mar
tin landed the 4 to 1 chance. Ferrier,
first, half a length ahead of the 15 to 1
shot, Fig Leaf.
There was nothing to the five furlong
scramble for maidens but Flushington,
the Corrigan entry. Martin, who had tha
mount, outrode his company, winning rid
den out from. Sevoy, the favorite.
Again, in the fourth event, a six furlong
spin, Martin's jockeyship was in evidence.
He tossed the leg over Tom Ryan's Don't
Skip Me, beating a speedy lot of
"lobsters." Pat Morrissey took the place
from Midlight.
Purser's Key del Tierra, ruled an odds
on choice for the mile selling run, and had
no difficulty in disposing of Hardly at the
end. Jerry Hunt set a hot pace out in
front to tne stretch, and then finished
third.
The mile and a quarter handicap went
to the Corrigan entry. Sardonic. Mc-
Nichols, who sat astride the mare, waited
until the stretch was reached, when he
moved up, winning handily a neck In ad
vance of Morellito, the favorite. Red
Glenn was a head farther away.

OAKLAND, Jan. 11.â Rev. James
B. McNally is to celebrate his
silver jubilee as a priest to
. morrow. Twenty-five years ago
Father McNally was ordained, and
since that time he has served in
several parishes, but has been in
West Oakland longer than any
other charge. His parishioners have
arranged a programme of exercises
to fittingly celebrate the occasion.
At 9 o'clock high mass will be cele
brated in St. Patrick's Church, and
in the evening the choir will have
Its pastor for its guest at a ban
quet In the new auditorium. The
exercises attending the banquet
will be rendered by seventy of the
young people of the church.
Father McNally has long been
recognized as one of the most
broadminded theologians in Ala
meda County. He has never been
backward In doing everything in
his power to forward local im
provements and propositions tend
ing to benefit this city. In addition
to his duties of the priesthood he
Is considerably interested In a
model ranch which he owns away
up in the Fruitvale Hills. This
ranch is known on account of many
experiments in agriculture which
Father McNally has made. For
Brandes Arraigned in Court.
OAKLAND, Jan. 11.â W. A. Brandes,
charged with brutally beating to death
his 16-year-old daughter, Lillian, was ar
raigned In Judge Hall's court to-day to
pleud, but on motion of Attorney F. W.
Sawyer, for the defense, the matter went
over for one week. Prison life seems to
have made but iutle difference la
Brandes' appearance, save that he haa
several weeks' growth of beard. Other
wise he appears hearty and contented.

TEACHERS IN DIRE TERROR
CONSOLIDATION AND BUSINESS
BASIS THE WATCHWOBDS.
A "Ladies' Night" Before the Board,
Whereat Momentous Changes
Were Inaugurated ,and
More Hinted At.
The Board of Education had a good
house last evening. It was the new mem
bers' second performance and the fame
of their first appearance brought the pub
lic to their doors. Long before thÂ«
Directors issued from their dressing-room
in the secretary's office every seat In the
Srallery HJid on the floor was occupied and
standing- room was at a discount.
It was really "lady teachers' night,"
as almost two-thirds of the large audi
ence were of the fair gender. Reclasslfl
cation and consolidation were in the air,
and earh teacher in the audience was
oppressed with the feeling that her posi
tion was slipping from under her. There
â were eleven Directors present and there
couid lie no luck in odd numbers in this |
instance, besides two secretaries were in
Attendance and that made thirteen, and
trouble was "on deck." Moreover, from i
the scattering of attorneys looming In I
tha crowd It was evident that somebody ;
w.-t* [ reparing to checkmate declared
vacancies with injunction suits.
In fact one injunction flitted into tha
chamber before the board was called to j
order. It was an order from Judge Daln- \
fcerneld enjoining the abolishment of Mis-s |
Kmfly l>yer's position as teacher. Miss
Dyer was assigned to the Washington
evening school without any class upon
â¢which to exercise her pedagogic privilege, !
and naturally anticipated trouble. Ac
cording to reports Washington evening
Â«chool is a "soft" old thing where teach
ers have from four to forty pupils to a
class.
The first act of the session last even-
Ing was the re-transference of Mrs. Mlch
ner back to Winneld Scott Grammar
school. The old board had sent her chas
ing a will o" the wisp class in the Spring
Valley Bchool, which of course she was
unablf to ratch. Miss Kate McGaugh was
by the old board sent somewhere, and
was last evening brought back to her
original position in the Hancock Gram
mar.
Director Stow asked to have the billet
of Miss Catherine Black, music teacher
at thp Lowell High School, sponged from
the blackboard. His request was granted
by his fellow Directors. Miss E. C. Finks
was a teacher at the Girls' High School
without a class. By the vote of the
her position went glimmering and
the salary was "unassigned."
Miss Ellen A. Sleator, through her at
torney, protested against the loss of her
class and that of three other teachers
In the IJncoln Primary School. She

The following is the report received on

new Mission High School building:
The system is still In an incomplete state.
No provisions have been made In any of thÂ»

openings have been made in any of the first
story rooms. In the Assembly room nothing
has been done for either heating or ventilating.
Two large openings In the wall were left above
the gallery for hot air which have been plas
tered up since the walla were finished and tint
ed. Where openings are made In rooms they
are still left with no registers or guards. The
basement Is badly cut up with ducts and par

than five feet is left to pass under them. The
delay in the work has. and is, causing much
annoyance to the occupant?, the Incomplete
fr.vstpm making It too hot In some rooms and
too cool In others. Teachers are obliged to use
windows to ventilate In all first and second
floor rooms.
The Board decided that the contractors
be given Uve days to complete this work;
in the event of failure in this the work
would be completed by the department.

GASTO N ASHE
ON EVILS OF
INTIMIDATION
Election Day Stories
Retold.
POLICE FAVORED DENNERY
WOULD NOT SUPPRESS HIS VIL
LAINOUS FOLLOWING.
The Contest for Porter Ashe's Seat
in the Senate to Be Transferred
Shortly to the State
Capitol.
The Dennery-Ashe election contest in- !
volving the right of Porter Ashe to a seat
in the State Senate is running its course
rapidly before Justices Kerrigan and j
Groezlnger. Three witnesses were ex- |
amined yesterday on behalf of Mr. Ashe,
ard it Is expected that all the testimony
will be in to-day. The witnesses yester
day were Sylvester M. Sullivan, Gaston
Ashe and Charles W. Freese, a personal !
friend of the Ashe boys.
They repeated the stories as to the
conduct of Dennery's followers through
out election dayâ of the prizefighters and j
negroes who went about Intimidating
voters and of the passive stand taken by
the police. Gaston Ashe told of Sergeant i
Shea being within fifteen feet of one man !
who was knocked down and declining to
make an arrest on the plea that he did !
not see the assault. He also declined to
remove a man who was creating a dis
turbance in a booth and who was working
for Dennery.
He testified to having met Sergeant j
Martin just after Porter Ashe had spoken j
harshly of the police at a meeting in Met
ropolitan Hall. Martin complained that
Ashe had given him an unmerited roast.
The latter retorted that Martin was seen
In Dennerv's company the previous night.
Martin acknowledged he had treated Den
nery to a glass of beer and volunteered- to
buy wine for Ashe. The offer was de
clined.
According to Gaston Ashe a desperate
crowd gathered in the booth at Pacific
and Taylor streets. They were not resi
dents of the neighborhood. They were
continually asking the tally clerk why
he did not put down more votes for Den
nery. This he could not do, owing to the
watchfulness of Gaston Ashe. The lat
ter, who was a champion boxer, and is big
and rugged, testified that he did not fear
bodily narm, but he took the precaution
to keep a table between himself and the
roughs, po that he could have plenty of
elbow room. He did not talk back or re
sent anything that was said to him. as he
thought "they might wipe up the floor
with me, and I wanted to remain and
protect my brother's interests."
The witness said they did not make a
saloon fight. He naively said that any
workers who wanted money were sent to
the other candidate's camp.
He said some car-drivers were friendly
to his brother, and Eugene Bert, for Den
nery, thought he had struck a lead into
the Southern Pacific Btronghold, hut It
did not prove bo. The men were gripmen
on the union-street line.
Sullivan, the first witness of the day,
saw an Ashe ballot counted for Dennery,
but upon his protest it was corrected. He
told of men who said they had to pre
tend to stand In with Dennery or the po
lice would arrest them as vagrants. Tne
case goes on this morning.

Lecture on the Philippines
Professor Davidson delitered a very in
teresting lecture on the Philippine Islands
at the Academy of Sciences last evening.
During his discourse he touched upon the
commercial merits of the group, and said
he believed the United States should have
controlled stations and lands in the Pa
cific years ago. The purchase of Alaska,
he claimed, was the first step taken to
ward making our nation a controller of
the world, and he hoped to see the
day when there should be no limit to tÂ«r

most of his time to a description of the
population, physical conditions, climate
and productions of these lands.

Professor Argenti, professor of micro

of the California Camera Club last even
ing. By means of lenses he produced on
canvascross and longitudinal sections of
! plants, flowers and rocks. The lenses used

Death of Mrs. Page.
Mrs. Hamilton Page, wife of Hamilton
Page, the managing owner of the Indian
apolis Furniture Company, died yesterday
morning at her home, 201 Buchanan street,
after a long and painful Illness. She
leaves one daughter, aged 15. The funeral
will take place this afternoon at 3 o'clock
from the family residence. The Interment
will be in Masonic Cemetery.

HOBSDN GREETED
BY THE HAWAIIANS
Warmly Welcomed at
Honolulu.
HUNDREDS SHAKE HIS HAND
TENDERED SEVERAL PUBLIC
RECEPTIONS.
Delivers a Speech, at the Dock, Refer
ring to the Navy's Euthusiasm
for Uncle Sam's
Cause.
HONOLULU. Jan. 4.â Lieutenant
Hobson, the hero of the Merrimac,
passed through here on December 31 on
his way to join the Manila fleet. Soon
after he came ashore from the Gaelic
he was taken to the Pali to see one of
the most striking pieces of nature's
handiwork and the no less striking con
quest of man over nature's difficulties,
the Pali road. He was entertained at
luncheon by Special Agent and Mrs.
Sewall at the American legation. There
was an impromptu public reception at
the Young Men's Christian Association
rooms and later at the Officers' Club
and a popular demonstration at the
wharf.
Hundrpds gathered at the wharf to
see the naval hero. He arrived there
in company with Mr. Sewall not ten
minutes before the steamer sailed.
Dense as the crowd was, a passage
through was kept clear. As he passed
; to the gang plank hundreds extended
their hands to shake his. Calm and
courteous, he recognized all greetings.
! Several ladies whom he met during the
day presented hi.a with lels.
As soon as he reached the deck the
1 order to cast off was given. From the
; bridge he acknowledged the continuous
' salutations and enthusiasm. To calls
for a speech he responded, hesitating
at first, but moved by the feelings to
which he gave expression, warmly and
fluently, as he thanked those present
| for the greeting they gave, and
; sketched briefly and enthusiastically
the spirit and acts of those who took
part with him in the Merrimac exploit |
and of the thousands of others who
sought so hard to be chosen to take
| part in it.
Writs of mandamus have been issued I
by the Circuit Court directing Collector
General McStocker to issue Hawaiian |
j registry to the bark Willscot and the!
ship Euterpe. The complainant is Col
onel George W. Macfarlane, and in the
case of the Euterpe Lincoln D. Spencer j
of Seattle, by his attorneys, Kinney & ;
Ballou and McClanahan. In the case I
of the Willscot temporary registry was !
issued by Consul Wilder at San Fran
cisco July 22.
The reason registry is refused is that
since the decision in the Chinese ha
beas corpus case on the question of sov
ereignty, the local government feels
j that the former decision under which |
registry was granted to the Kilohana j
! and other vessels has been modified and
| it has been decided to bring the ques
tion anew before the courts for further
consideration.
Application for Hawaiian registry has
been made for the Star of France.

hala on Tuesday, December 26, and the i

Four of the round-the-Horn sugar !
fleet are In port. They are the St. i
